```
>tables are features that I personally think would be very useful! 

The average Cobol programmer has avoided Occurs Depending On for 22 years. It's unlikely
he or she will use any-length items and dynamic-capacity tables. 

This may be a clue to Micro Focus' position:
"Some OO programming languages perform garbage collection at run time, that is, they
automatically destroy objects that are no longer in use. OO COBOL does not provide garbage
collection."

Server Express v5 manual
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2008-03-11T13:22:28-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<O5ABj.62244$Pv2.27356@newssvr23.news.prodigy.net>`
- **References:** `<HAXAj.5290$FE.820@fe05.news.easynews.com> <47D56BE4.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <m1pbt3hf9l197putsqlr2pvr1iccpgv7ka@4ax.com> <dDzBj.440419$Ib6.70764@fe03.news.easynews.com>`

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote in message 
news:dDzBj.440419>
> [progams]  that require ODO's, what he has stated does apply in some 
> environments, but requires a use of "average" that is not universally 
> used.

Occurs Depending On is a perfectly fine and potentially quite useful 
source-code level technique.

But it's like all other techniques: there are times to use it and times to 
use something else.

Trying to quantify the techniques used by the "average" programmer is a 
fool's errand. Example?

One time I was bought in as an outside contractor to work on a very specific 
piece of software with which I had prior experience. The person supervising 
my work had been the primary COBOL programmer for the client for nine (9) 
years.

When I pronounced the job done, quite reasonably this supervisor wanted to 
check out my coding techinques before she let me go.

Imagine my surprise when this nine-year veteran told me not only was my code 
acceptable, but she was glad to have had the chance to review it because she 
had NEVER SEEN THE 'SEARCH' VERB USED BEFORE.

That was the day I quit making *any* assumptions about "normal" or "average" 
programming techniques.
```

```
<cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:

>>But that's only my experience, I can't speak for the industry.
>I believe that OCCURS DEPENDING ON is useful for tables that can have
>a variable number of entries and are in sequence for searching using
>SEARCH ALL.

It can save a search over my using a big table with high value records
at the end.   On the other hand, it adds overhead as the run-time
program figures out sizes.

I suspect the savings you might think is there is illusory.
```

- **References:** `<HAXAj.5290$FE.820@fe05.news.easynews.com> <47D56BE4.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <m1pbt3hf9l197putsqlr2pvr1iccpgv7ka@4ax.com> <dDzBj.440419$Ib6.70764@fe03.news.easynews.com>`

```
On Tue, 11 Mar 2008 17:50:02 GMT, "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
>news:m1pbt3hf9l197putsqlr2pvr1iccpgv7ka@4ax.com...
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>that of those programs and programmers that I have seen, that ..."

When ODO comes up in this forum, as it did 3 years ago and 6 months ago in my Myth Busters
thread, some here said it should not be used because it is too slow (even when faced with
contrary evidence)  or because it adds 'unnecessary complexity'. The Micro Focus Server
Express performance tips manual says it is slower; an IBM manual you linked to says the
same. 

ODO is one of many faith-based  issues in the Cobol community.

Manuals fail to point out that ODO makes SEARCH ALL 10% faster for typical (2^10) table
size. Assuming the table is half full, the difference is 1 - (log2(n) / (1 + log2(n))). 

>Needless to say, given the LARGE number of files that are still used by COBOL 
>programs and that require ODO's, what he has stated does apply in some 
>environments, but requires a use of "average" that is not universally used.

Files? I thought files were replaced by databases years ago. Rows don't contain arrays.
```

```
> because it is too slow (even when faced with
> contrary evidence)  or because it adds 'unnecessary complexity'. The Micro Focus Server
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> ODO is one of many faith-based  issues in the Cobol community.

Here is a clip from an HP3000 manual:

>   *   Access to tables defined with
>       OCCURS...DEPENDING is less efficient than access to tables of
>       fixed size, and so should be avoided where high performance is
>       needed.

This seems fairly straight forward. Why do you think that HP lies
about this ?


> Manuals fail to point out that ODO makes SEARCH
> ALL 10% faster for typical (2^10) table
> size. Assuming the table is half full, the difference is 1 - (log2(n) / (1 + log2(n))).
>

Why do you think that a table size of 4000 or so is 'typical' ?

Whether it makes the search faster or slower is dependent on the
implementation, as well as many other things.

The HP manual says that _access_ is slower. It may be that the SEARCH
ALL is faster, or maybe not. It may be that in many programs the
tables are not used for SEARCH ALL and so your tiny detail is
completely irrelevant.


> >Needless to say, given the LARGE number of files that are still used by COBOL

> >environments, but requires a use of "average" that is not universally used.
```

```
These are different things entirely. You are correct that with a
smaller table the number of binary chops reduces. However, if each
access is 'less efficient', because for example it checks against a
data item rather than literal, then it may still be slower.

In any case, even if the SEARCH ALL is faster it may be that loading
the table is slower, especially if the DO is incremented with each
access.
```

```
On Wed, 12 Mar 2008 11:30:03 -0700 (PDT), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Mar 13, 2:16 am, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Tue, 11 Mar 2008 20:38:59 -0700 (PDT), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>bother with it.

They say ODO is inefficient, which is Cobolese for slow. When I presented evidence that
ODO is not slow, they criticized my test as inadequate because it conflicted with their
belief. Now, when I say they are opposed to ODO, that too is wrong. 

You can't have it both ways.

>> >Whether it makes the search faster or slower is dependent on the
>> >implementation, as well as many other things.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>data item rather than literal, then it may still be slower.

A binary search does not check its internal pointer against a table limit on each
iteration. When it starts, it LOADS a lower and upper bound from either a data item or
literal. Thereafter, there is no reason to check limits. 

>In any case, even if the SEARCH ALL is faster it may be that loading
>the table is slower, especially if the DO is incremented with each
>access.

You are ASSuming bounds checking is on and bounds checking uses the DO item. Micro Focus
does not use the DO item, it uses the maximum, which is a literal.
```

```
"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
news:fk0ht3di28s4e20332rrgn566tb4fumel4@4ax.com...
> On Wed, 12 Mar 2008 11:30:03 -0700 (PDT), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
<snip>
> They say ODO is inefficient, which is Cobolese for slow. When I presented 
> evidence that
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Robert,
 "Having it both ways" is EXACTLY what I think the case is.  There are shops and 
programmers where ODO's are still widely used (in File section if not 
Working-Storage, Local-Storage, and Linkage Section).  There are also shops and 
programmers where they are either never used or very close to it.

My original problem was you assuming that you knew what the "average" programmer 
currently used (and has been using for the last 22 years).

I certainly do NOT know the answer to "what percentage" of programmers use ODO's
 - ever
 - never
 - occassionally
 - only in File Section (if they still use files at all <G>)
 - etc

The statement that (like paragraphs/sections, periods within procedures, etc) 
this is an issue where styles differ.  It is also completely true that there are 
POTENTIAL performance issues related to ODO's and probably NOT "storage 
benefits - except in File Section.   That the application design,  operating 
system and compiler, use of indexing/subscripting/whatever, how the data comes 
in, what the average size of the table is, etc ALL impact whether the use of an 
ODO *might* be appropriate for a specific application. It certainly (as far as I 
know NEVER required - except when interacting with another program or file that 
defines a structure)
```

```
On Mar 13, 2:44 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> On Wed, 12 Mar 2008 11:30:03 -0700 (PDT), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
> >On Mar 13, 2:16 am, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:

> They say ODO is inefficient, which is Cobolese for slow.

NO. They said _ACCESS_ was inefficient.

> When I presented evidence that
> ODO is not slow, they criticized my test as inadequate because it conflicted with their
> belief. Now, when I say they are opposed to ODO, that too is wrong.

You presented just _one_ aspect of ODO, that of a SEARCH ALL. You did
not time any other access to the table.  I found that it was
consistently 1% slower for simple MOVEs.

ODO was used primarily for data records where the actual record size
is controlled by the DO. What testing did you do with this ?  Perhaps
it is 'inefficient' to MOVE records between W-S and FD record areas
when there is an ODO.

They didn't say it made SEARCH ALL 'inefficient'.

> You can't have it both ways.

You are taking a very narrow view. Probably because yopu have this
crusade where you want to 'prove' that everything vendors and
'ignorant mainframers' say is 'wrong' and everything that you say is
'right'.

ODO used for file systems, record areas, and associated W-S moves may
well be inefficient, and also may well be a bad thing suitable for
banning from code.

ODO may well be useful for SEARCH ALL ('74) and table SORT ('02).
While it _may_ make SEARCH ALL save an access or two, if the
consequence is that _all_ other accesses are slower then it is not
useful.

In any case SEARCH ALL seems not to be widely used.


> >> >Whether it makes the search faster or slower is dependent on the
> >> >implementation, as well as many other things.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> literal. Thereafter, there is no reason to check limits.

I note your use of the imperitive "does not" (rather than, say, need
not).
You've obviously examined the output of every single compiler ever
written in order to make this sweeping claim.

But, in any case, you have _completely_ missed the point. 'access' is
done when the program puts each data item into the table, or retrieves
it, or the SEARCH  compares the keys in the table entry against the
required value.


> >In any case, even if the SEARCH ALL is faster it may be that loading
> >the table is slower, especially if the DO is incremented with each
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> does not use the DO item, it uses the maximum, which is a literal.

No, I am not assuming anything.  I tested Fujitsu (no bound checks, no
DO changing) loading a table compared to a fixed size and found the
ODO was consistently slower, granted by just 1%. It is obviously using
a different instruction sequence somewhere.

Now logically I should change the DO every time I add an item to the
table and this, in itself, could make it slower.

Of course the real overhead of using ODO comes when there are multiple
or nested ODOs and then access is really complicated, and very
inefficient.

Also Micro Focus may be your whole world, but others use different
compilers which may well have different performance characteristics.
```

```
On Mar 14, 8:41 am, Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>

Further to that the test was doing just one MOVE in each iteration of
stepping through the table.  This meant that most of the time in the
tests was exactly the same set of PERFORM loops. By changing it to
several MOVEs in each iteration I got a consistent 8% difference:

+00003910   ODO
+00003654   fixed occurs

Thus my actual tests _PROVE_, for the Fujitsu COBOL that I use, that
the MF claim of "access is less efficient" is TRUE.

Not that 'SEARCH ALL saving an iteration or two' is not inconsistent
with 'access is less efficient'.

Robert's beliefs (and narrow view), in this case that "ODO is not
slow" is once again shown to be WRONG.
```

```
>efficient" as MF and HP claims.

A tester (experimenter) would measure the speed. A programmer (theoritician) would figure
it out with logic. As a programmer, I ask why WOULD it be slower? A reference to a
subscripted element has no reason to reference the DO item, only a reference to a group
name does. It gets the size from the DO item and multiplies by the reach of an occurrance.
That's two instructions. The alternative is to load the length from a literal. Time for
the extra instruction is small compared to the time to execute the move. 

>> >Not that 'SEARCH ALL saving an iteration or two' is not inconsistent
>> >with 'access is less efficient'.

Complex keys is babbling. A SEARCH ALL looks for one key, with an equal comparison.

The difference is not one or two chops (minimizing), it is a 10% reduction in search time.
If you didn't care about speed, you wouldn't be using SEARCH ALL in the first place, you'd
be using a serial SEARCH .. or be querying a file or database.

>> The proposition I tested was that subscript evaluation is slower for ODO.
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>thus your claim is wrong).

We're talking about table searches, yet your test did not measure the speed of table
searches. 

Do this:

Define a table with 1,000 entries.
Initialize it to high-values.
Populate 500 entries with data that's in sequence. 
Get the key of entry (250). 
Do a million SEARCH ALLs on that middle key without ODO. 
Do a million SEARCH ALLs on that middle key with ODO. 
Report the time of the first populate and million searches versus the second.
I predict the second will be 10% faster.
```

```
No. Wrong. An OCCURS clause can specify several keys, both ASCENDING

an 'equals'.


> The difference is not one or two chops (minimizing), it is a 10% reduction in search time.

of chops: zero, 1, 2, or so. If the DO makes the table half the total
size or less than you will on average save one chop.

Whether there is _any_ saving at all depends on how much 'less

actually less so, at least on my tests).

If the 'less efficient' is 10% and the 'saving' is 10% then the SEARCH
ALL will not be improved at all.

If you really cared about high speed you would use a hash table, which
potentially can be _much_ faster. Of course this may depend on
suitable keys or sufficiently low packing density.

optimised based on some history of previous usage so that high hit
rates are in early nodes.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 13)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-03-15T20:46:07-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<k6sot397qpmqmiqqb1veampbojkv4gjb1t@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<9c9f43b4-622c-431c-af76-f235c65015b0@s13g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <fk0ht3di28s4e20332rrgn566tb4fumel4@4ax.com> <f234e168-0774-46b9-afe7-9fb32376d5b6@i29g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <fdb338da-5ef5-4b95-af03-e834e9fe6df2@i12g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <72ijt3d13e2rvf0ha5u3b9fncvvr7m5h3e@4ax.com> <024ba9bd-51e9-4433-b34b-b541d02a9859@d4g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <1k6mt3hr864pk8co1j1orfm984j2lfqgvk@4ax.com> <18199eae-bffe-420c-8e32-cb6ebf7533c2@e25g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Fri, 14 Mar 2008 20:12:50 -0700 (PDT), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Mar 15, 2:52 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Thu, 13 Mar 2008 22:40:09 -0700 (PDT), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>> >On Mar 14, 6:37 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> >> On Thu, 13 Mar 2008 13:13:56 -0700 (PDT), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>> >> >On Mar 14, 8:41 am, Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>> >> >By changing it to
>> >> >several MOVEs in each iteration I got a consistent 8% difference:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>assumptions that it would not be 'less efficient'.

The difference would depend on the ratio of perform to move. Typically they're about the
same, so your 1% would become 2%.

One percent is a small difference. If you didn't do enough iterations, it might just
reflect measurement error.

>> >> It is easy to picture that happening, because the optimizer thinks the 1st or 2nd move
>> >> might have changed either the subscript or the DO item, so it recomputes the subscript for
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>compiler and stated that 'access is less efficient'.

Micro Focus' efficiency advice is exactly the same on every platform, therefore was NOT
based on tests run on that platform. They struck me as folklore, maxims widely believed in
the Cobol community, but simply not true. I picked the ones that sounded wrong and tested
them. Results showed Micro Focus' advice was wrong in some cases. Specifically:

Indexes are faster than subscripts. 
  They are actually slower.
  This is a frequently asked question during technical interviews. Telling 
  them they're all wet will cause you to fail. You must lie better than the competition.

Simple two-operand arithmetic statements are faster than COMPUTE.  
   Sometimes true, sometimes the same. The problem is caused by intermediate 
  results having needlessly high precision. That's because compiler writers don't 
  grok number theory.

Do not use the REMAINDER, ROUNDED, ON SIZE ERROR or CORRESPONDING. 
  False for rounded; true for on size error. I didn't test remainder and corresponding 
 because it's obvious the advice is false.

To execute a loop n times, set the counter to n and then decrement the counter until it
becomes zero, rather than incrementing the counter from zero to n. 
  Turned out to be true. This is based on machine language instructons that loop until 
  a counter goes to zero. It is not a general truism because some machines have no
  such instruction. 

Packed decimal arithmetic is slower than binary.
   I did not doubt this, wanted to see how much slower. Results showed packed is about
  as slow as display (on non-mainframe).

Alignment on memory boundaries matters (generally on RISC machines)
  True. I thought the L2 cache would make alignment a non-issue. I was wrong. Alignment 
  matters a lot more than I thought 

>> The difference is not one or two chops (minimizing), it is a 10% reduction in search time.
>
>The reduction for a single SEARCH ALL can only be an integral number
>of chops: zero, 1, 2, or so. If the DO makes the table half the total
>size or less than you will on average save one chop.

True, but one chop is 10% of ten chops for table size between 513 and 1,024. 

>Whether there is _any_ saving at all depends on how much 'less
>efficient' the access actually is (now that I have show that it is
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>ALL will not be improved at all.

There is no reason to think looks inside a SEARCH would be slowed by ODO. 

>> If you didn't care about speed, you wouldn't be using SEARCH ALL in the first place, you'd
>> be using a serial SEARCH .. or be querying a file or database.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>suitable keys or sufficiently low packing density.

That's what Computer Scientists say. Micro Focus' collection and dictionary classes use
hashing. They are more than 100 times slower than doing it yourself. 

>Or one could implement a binary tree in a table. A tree could be
>optimised based on some history of previous usage so that high hit
>rates are in early nodes.

Yes. It is common for hits to have a chi-squared distribution, called the '80/20 rule' in
informal language. Under the right conditions, even a serial search can be faster than a
divisional search. One can improve speed by adding dimensions to the table space. Instead
of a single table, there can be one table holding frequently used, sometimes called a
cache, and a second table containing all the rest. 

>> >> The proposition I tested was that subscript evaluation is slower for ODO.
>>
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>That would give a single chop, real good sample testing.

OK, select the search subjects randomly.

>> Do a million SEARCH ALLs on that middle key without ODO.
>> Do a million SEARCH ALLs on that middle key with ODO.
>> Report the time of the first populate and million searches versus the second.
>> I predict the second will be 10% faster.

Add a third test that inserts the table into an indexed file and does the lookups with
file reads. I predict it will be more than 10 times slower.
```

```
"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
news:k6sot397qpmqmiqqb1veampbojkv4gjb1t@4ax.com...

>  They are actually slower.

You cannot say that (and have it considered as credible) without 
qualification. Sometimes they are; sometimes they're not.

Mostly they are quicker because they don't require conversion to an offset. 
(Again, it depends on how the vendor has implemented it.)

>  This is a frequently asked question during technical interviews. Telling
>  them they're all wet will cause you to fail. You must lie better than the 
> competition.

:-)

>
> Simple two-operand arithmetic statements are faster than COMPUTE.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>  grok number theory.

Yes, a fair statement ... if a little unkind to compiler writers... :-)
>
> Do not use the REMAINDER, ROUNDED, ON SIZE ERROR or CORRESPONDING.
>  False for rounded; true for on size error. I didn't test remainder and 
> corresponding
> because it's obvious the advice is false.

You live in a wonderful world, Robert :-).
>
> To execute a loop n times, set the counter to n and then decrement the 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>  such instruction.

Again this will depend on whether you are using TEST BEFORE (the default) or 
TEST AFTER. And the "gain" is not more than 2 or 3 millisesonds on any 
modern platform (much less than that on most)
>
> Packed decimal arithmetic is slower than binary.
>   I did not doubt this, wanted to see how much slower. Results showed 
> packed is about
>  as slow as display (on non-mainframe).

Again, requires qualification. Depends on platform, hardware, type of 
arithmetic being done, and implementation. However, generally true, yes.

>
> Alignment on memory boundaries matters (generally on RISC machines)
>  True. I thought the L2 cache would make alignment a non-issue. I was 
> wrong. Alignment
>  matters a lot more than I thought

And it SHOULD be transparent to the COBOL programmer.
>
>>> The difference is not one or two chops (minimizing), it is a 10% 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>>actually less so, at least on my tests).

This is SO silly. (10%, 20%, even 90% saved on something that is VERY small 
to begin with, is just not worth arguing about). If you saved 1 single disk 
access, you would have covered your SEARCH saving around 50 times over...

>>
>>If the 'less efficient' is 10% and the 'saving' is 10% then the SEARCH
>>ALL will not be improved at all.

Exactly.

>
> There is no reason to think looks inside a SEARCH would be slowed by ODO.

I think there is.

>
>>> If you didn't care about speed, you wouldn't be using SEARCH ALL in the 
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> hashing. They are more than 100 times slower than doing it yourself.

Doesn't that mean that it is unwise to make blanket, unqualified statements 
in this area?

>
>>Or one could implement a binary tree in a table. A tree could be
…[54 more quoted lines elided]…
> file reads. I predict it will be more than 10 times slower.

A pointless pissing contest. (Even if you were right... :-))

I notice you are happy to direct someone else to do these dubious tests, but 
you haven't done them yourself...:-)

I don't know (I haven't tried it....) but it is PROBABLY possible to 
disprove almost any sweeping statement about COBOL performance that is 
unqualified. Why anyone'd want to do that, I have no idea. You can bet that 
under SOME set of conditions, on SOME platform, using SOME compiler options 
and SOME particular version of the compiler, the generalised rule will be 
seen to NOT be true.

So what? Do we need absolutes when it comes to COBOL performance? It's not 
like a parachute that almost opens or a boat that almost floats... Get it 
wrong, and the three hour invoicing run takes 20 milliseconds longer...

There is very little you can do to a reasonably well written COBOL program 
(that has followed the Guidelines), to optimise it in memory, that can't be 
trumped 50 fold by optimising its IO.

LRMs when talking about performance, are usually quite clear that what they 
are saying is a "GENERAL GUIDELINE" (a bit like "The Code" in "Pirates of 
the Caribbean").

If each entry in a memory table is subject to an ODO, and the table itself 
is also subject to ODO, then it seems pretty obvious that access will be 
slower than it would be with fixed length entries and table (There is 
(probably) an ODO calculation overhead on every access). On the other hand, 
if only the table itself is subject to ODO there MAY be a small saving 
because the number of chops can be smaller on a sparsely populated table.

But I'm pretty sure it isn't going to be a great peformance enhancer. After 
switching the table to ODO, I don't think anyone is going to say:"Wow! look 
at those invoices flying out...Just as well we switched to ODO..."

Responsible vendors try to ensure that people know about their approaches 
and can comprehend and utilise them. They also know that many people just 
want some simple rules which, if applied, in most cases, will give better 
performance.

I see no harm in that.

Pete.
```

```
>> Packed decimal arithmetic is slower than binary.
>>   I did not doubt this, wanted to see how much slower. Results showed 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>arithmetic being done, and implementation. However, generally true, yes.

Binary integer arithmetic is 100% accurate. The difference between binary and decimal only
shows up when dealing with fractions. Most business applications deal ONLY with integers.
Currency amounts are not fractions, they are cents counters. Did you know Cobol allows V
in the picture of binary integers? Cobol does the decimal shifting and alignment for you,
WITHOUT causing rounding errors. 

In short, we never needed packed decimal. It was a marketing ploy.

>> There is no reason to think looks inside a SEARCH would be slowed by ODO.
>
>I think there is.

Why? There are no holes in arrays.

>are saying is a "GENERAL GUIDELINE" (a bit like "The Code" in "Pirates of 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>slower than it would be with fixed length entries and table 

The Cobol standard says that's not allowed. 

>(There is  (probably) an ODO calculation overhead on every access). On the other hand, 
>if only the table itself is subject to ODO there MAY be a small saving 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>I see no harm in that.

Here's a simple rule: every table that's SEARCHed should be defined with ODO. 

Seems pretty simple to me. :)
```

```
On Mon, 17 Mar 2008 14:18:33 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>In article <m8sst3lq5qnfb7qdh19mgvj757uupegb50@4ax.com>,
>Robert  <no@e.mail> wrote:
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>please address this.

Currencies used to be fractional, when there were units worth 1/12 of a zloty and 1/8 of a
dong. Since 1974, all currencies, without exception, have been decimal. Therefore, there
is no more reason to store full stops than there is to store commas every three digits, or
vice versa. 

One of the first things a programmer is taught is to avoid premature rounding or
truncation. Internally, computers keep the smallest currency unit, such as cents. They
scale to whole, thousands or millions of kwanzas when editing a number for human
consumption.

If you insist on premature scaling, Cobol provides picture P.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 13:26:02 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>In article <d8jvt3h4d0k9nj0r5hd8vsr1gus3fnc6sr@4ax.com>,

…[52 more quoted lines elided]…
>There's a clever lad... thanks for admitting to that particular error.

It says the small units are no longer used.

>>>>Therefore, there
>>>>is no more reason to store full stops than there is to store commas
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
>units these fractions are decimal fractions'?  If so, please address this.

Cobol doesn't  measure US currency in dollars and fractions of a dollar, it  measures in
cents. If you looked at my hourly rate, $12.34, in a Cobol file or in memory, you would
see either "00123D", "001234C" or x"+0004D2". You would see no evidence of fractions. 

Picture character V causes those numbers to be CONVERTED to dollars and fractions of a
dollar. It doesn't change their fundamental unit of measure. 

Other languages measure currency in dollars by the use of a decimal point or floating
point exponent. My comment was in the context of Cobol, since this is a Cobol forum.
```

```
On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 08:15:40 -0600, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:

>I already addressed it by pointing out that Cobol lets you use picture V in binary
>integers, thus eliminating the imagined  need for comp-3. 

Was there ever a "need" to use packed-decimal?   In my experience it
has always been a matter of knowing what is most efficient to use with
the system I'm using.

The language provided me options - I was supposed to pick the option
that fit the system I was working with that best fit the requirements
of my employer.
```

```
On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 12:31:10 -0600, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:

with display on IBM mainframes.     It's also easier to read in a dump

decision - which doesn't mean it isn't sometimes the best decision.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-03-18T12:32:50-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7840e91e-23f9-4148-bbc0-2dd285751172@s8g2000prg.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<f234e168-0774-46b9-afe7-9fb32376d5b6@i29g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <m8sst3lq5qnfb7qdh19mgvj757uupegb50@4ax.com> <frlujp$60u$1@reader2.panix.com> <g84ut3ptdm6jqvebea78gtd921mm7n04ch@4ax.com> <fro1fs$579$1@reader2.panix.com> <d8jvt3h4d0k9nj0r5hd8vsr1gus3fnc6sr@4ax.com> <n7mvt39m1rspj9n2nnuf62vv1i1vja5mn0@4ax.com> <ke20u3poj7beae01khg0kiheq2spch5p4e@4ax.com>`

```
On Mar 19, 7:31 am, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 09:02:56 -0600, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> >On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 08:15:40 -0600, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> On which mainframe was binary slower than packed decimal?

IBM 1401

You are limiting yourself by only being able to consider simple
arithmetic operations. It may well be that on most machines adding,
multiplying and such on 32bit binary are faster. However for
commercial work data input, print output and display is just as
important.

Converting between display and packed for large numbers with scaling
was likely to be faster than the conversion for binary.

Another issue is that hardware support for binary was only 32 bit (or
24 on ICL 1900, I still remember 8,388,607) and this was inadequate.
Larger binary required code routines to deal with these numbers which
was considerably slower.  Hardware support for packed may have been
slower for small numbers but it overtook the software support where
accounting sized numbers were used, and trounced it on converting to
display for printing.

It was possible to have unaligned or part word binary but this added
the overhead of unpacking and repacking the data for each operation.

> >The language provided me options - I was supposed to pick the option
> >that fit the system I was working with that best fit the requirements
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> binary?

Packed also could be written to tape using byte size increments to
better utilise capacity. Binary 32 bit or 64 bit (software supported)
would actually waste space.

Having done tests back in the late 70s comparing Cobol packed, binary
and display calculations and _including_ formatting for input,
printing and display for typical applications (eg invoicing) the
packed was actually just ahead of binary. Obviously as more complex
calculations were required then the binary may have won.

Today there is hardware support for 64 bit and that makes a
significant difference, one that 30 or 40 years ago was not available.
```

```
On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 09:02:56 -0600, Howard Brazee wrote:

> On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 08:15:40 -0600, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:
> 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> my employer.

Several programmers commented to me that they found packed decimal a lot
easier to read in memory dumps than binary. Also, in some cases, the
hardware supports larger numbers in packed decimal than in binary, so
large numbers can be more efficient in packed decimal than in binary.
Finally, packed decimal provides an exception if uninitialized data is
used, because all binary zeros is not a valid packed decimal value. In
contrast, binary zeros is a valid binary value. So the risk of silently
using uninitialized data is lower.

Tim
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 23)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-03-18T22:46:27+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7DXDj.119908$I5.105779@fe09.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<f234e168-0774-46b9-afe7-9fb32376d5b6@i29g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <m8sst3lq5qnfb7qdh19mgvj757uupegb50@4ax.com> <frlujp$60u$1@reader2.panix.com> <g84ut3ptdm6jqvebea78gtd921mm7n04ch@4ax.com> <fro1fs$579$1@reader2.panix.com> <d8jvt3h4d0k9nj0r5hd8vsr1gus3fnc6sr@4ax.com> <n7mvt39m1rspj9n2nnuf62vv1i1vja5mn0@4ax.com>`

```
First of all "COMP-3" is an extension.  Packed-Decimal is a Standard USAGE 
introduced in the '85 Standard.

For IBM mainframe COBOL compilers, there have always been performance 
information that shows that the use of its extension of COMP-3 will perform 
better for SOME cases with mixed usages and requiring multiple conversions.

Until the '02 Standard, COBOL never had a "true binary" data type.  It always 
used a base-10 truncation, rounding, and on size categorization for binary data 
items.  In many cases, when running in "standard conforming" mode, this causes 
some performance problems - and some problems with communicating with other 
languages and systems that supported "true binary" data types.

  ***

None of the discussion so far as addressed the S/3, S/36, S/38, OS/400, iSeries 
environment where it is my understanding that packed decimal STILL performs as 
well or better than binary for most arithmetic operations.
```

```
…[51 more quoted lines elided]…
> You mean nanoseconds. A millisecond is a LOONG time.

Yes, I actually meant microseconds, but it could well be nanoseconds on 
modern processors.

>
>>> Packed decimal arithmetic is slower than binary.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Binary integer arithmetic is 100% accurate.

I know. I once wrote a program for system 360 that used ONLY binary 
arithmetic (no packed). To everyone's amazement, it produced correct printed 
figures...


The difference between binary and decimal only
> shows up when dealing with fractions. Most business applications deal ONLY 
> with integers.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> In short, we never needed packed decimal. It was a marketing ploy.

Hmmm... not sure about that. There had been some famous errors with floating 
point and I think that people were looking for the reassurance of fixed 
point arithmetic.

>
>>> There is no reason to think looks inside a SEARCH would be slowed by 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Why? There are no holes in arrays.

If each entry is governed by ODO there may well be an access overhead. It 
means indirect addressing and offset calculation which may not be required 
if the entry length is fixed with an index. Again, it really depends on the 
particular implementation.
>
>>I don't know (I haven't tried it....) but it is PROBABLY possible to
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> advocate following them.

Er...can you show where I said: "generalizations are useless"?

I said that your particular sweeping statements are problematic, and I 
explained why I believe that. I certainly never said that all 
generalizations are useless; that, in itself, would be a useless 
generalization...:-)

The generalizations provided as guidelines in the LRM are QUALIFIED as such. 
That makes them far from useless.
>
>>LRMs when talking about performance, are usually quite clear that what 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> The Cobol standard says that's not allowed.

I could be wrong, but I seem to recall having seen code where it was 
implemented. It may have been an extension. As I don't use it myself and as 
I don't really care about ODO, I didn't take as much notice as I might have. 
(It was also 20 years ago...).

If it is not allowed, then much of what you are saying is probably fair 
comment. If there is only ONE ODO and that is the overall one for the table, 
then there may indeed be a gain in using ODO on a LARGE table. (I actually 
never said there wasn't; I said your remarks were "interesting".)

However, it is a very small gain, and more than outweighed by the ugliness 
of ODO, in my opinion.
>
>>(There is  (probably) an ODO calculation overhead on every access). On the 
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> ODO.

No. You MIGHT formulate a rule where every table that's SEARCHed ALL should 
be defined with ODO.
>
> Seems pretty simple to me. :)

Sure, you could make that a rule, if you got agreement from the people who 
have to use it. I would vote against it, but I'd go with the flow if the 
majority were OK with it. I don't see the "savings" as being important 
enough to get worked up about.

Pete.
```

```
01  odo-area.
     05  odo-entry occurs 1 to 1000 depending on do pic x.
          
move odo-entry (2) to ....

Odo-entry (2) is at odo-area + 1, same as it would be if there were no odo. 

>> Here's a simple rule: every table that's SEARCHed should be defined with 
>> ODO.
>
>No. You MIGHT formulate a rule where every table that's SEARCHed ALL should 
>be defined with ODO.

If you SEARCH serially and the subject is not in the table, there's no point in searching
100 high values at end end. You want to stop after the last entry loaded.
```

```
On Mon, 17 Mar 2008 20:09:47 -0700 (PDT), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Mar 18, 4:53 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 11:33:00 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>> >news:6dmrt3dn0hueihi3p996d5n96sts0pgtm1@4ax.com...

>> >> Here's a simple rule: every table that's SEARCHed should be defined with
>> >> ODO.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>searching for (given the entries in order).

If the table is in key order, you wouldn't be doing a serial search. There is a point
below which serial search is faster. I would hope SEARCH ALL is smart enough to switch to
serial at that point. 

-----

I just tested it with Fujitsu on Wintel. The crossover point is 7 entries. ALL does NOT
automatically switch below that point. At 3 entries, ALL is 13% slower. At 15, ALL is 25%
faster.
```

```
On Mar 19, 7:41 am, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 09:12:16 -0600, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
> >On Mon, 17 Mar 2008 22:53:26 -0600, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> own.

The compiler's conceptual weaknesses are those of the compiler
writer's, or his ability to implement within the constraints imposed
by the target machine.

Thus it is not anthropromorphizing.
```

```
"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message

really should read more carefully...).

I said that "people" were looking for reassurance... It is very reassuring 
(for some people) when a man with an IBM business card, in a dark suit and a 
white shirt gazes into your eyes and says: "Unlike the others, our company 
understnads you have quite proper concerns about using scientific arithmetic 
for calculating your invoices. We have a new DECIMAL option, specifically 
designed for dealing with currency, which will guarantee you can NEVER get a 
rounding error..."

>
> I'm but advocating binary floating point, but I'll point out that Excel 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> errors there?

Well occasionally we do... http://www.eusprig.org/stories.htm

But, there are functions (like ROUND) within Excel that are intended to deal 
with these problems. Unfortunately, many of the people writing Excel macros 
are NOT programmers and just do the best they can.
>
>>>>> There is no reason to think looks inside a SEARCH would be slowed by
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> That's simply not true.

Read it again. It says MAY not.


Consider:
>

OK, I considered it... :-)

> 01  odo-area.
>     05  odo-entry occurs 1 to 1000 depending on do pic x.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> odo.

Suppose it was odo-entry (X)? The offset must be calculated for both fixed 
and ODO, but the length of the move must also be calculated for ODO
>
>>> Here's a simple rule: every table that's SEARCHed should be defined with
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> 100 high values at end end. You want to stop after the last entry loaded.

The way I do it does exactly that.

LARGE tables would NOT be searched serially, as I stated.

Actually, I avoid all of this nonsense by loading the table in the required 
sequence. I also note the last active entry in the table to limit serial 
searches (if the table is small), and employ my own binary chop (which uses 
the last entry loaded as the upper limit) on very large tables. Doing this 
does not require the table to be defined with KEY IS and does not require 
the table to have ODO applied to it. I do NOT (as a matter of course; I do, 
on sites where the local standard mandates it and have done so in my own 
stuff occasionally to see how it worked...) use COBOL SEARCH ALL, although I 
probably should...

I should also note that when loading the table I do NOT "ripple down" 
entries below the one being inserted, thus generating dozens, maybe even 
hundreds or thousands of subscripted moves to work areas then back to the 
table... I use a single refmodded MOVE statement.

(In the old days, before we had refmodding, I used Assembler subroutines I 
wrote and called from COBOL on both the IBM Mainframe and the Intel x86 
platforms...) The function prototype was: Call 'AVMOVE' using SourceField, 
SourceFieldOffset, TargetField, TargetFieldOffset, Length. The original was 
written for System 360 which was "limited" by 256 bytes for a single MOVE 
instruction. This was revised to use extended register instructions for 
System 370, and REP MOVSD double word move for the Intel x86 platforms.

I did try (once...) using Boolean instructions to swap entries without 
having to use intermediate storage, but it was only marginally better and 
not easy to follow.

(You can swap two entries in memory, without the need for intermediate 
storage, by using XOR A->B, XOR B->A, XOR A->B, where you can read -> as 
"into", and XOR as "Exclusive OR"... Why this works, is a result of some 
interesting propositional calculus... :-))

The XOR solution was only marginally better than just moving stuff , so I 
finally settled on that, but using a single block move to "open up" the 
table for the new entry.

I therefore have no need whatsoever of ODO, it wouldn't have ANY advantage 
over what I do now, and, as I keep telling you, it is UGLY! :-)

Pete.
"I used to write COBOL...now I can do anythng."
```

```
On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 18:10:36 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
>news:6l7ut3h7qu4kt6bdkj7pl2ac0is7ut78ej@4ax.com...

>>>> Here's a simple rule: every table that's SEARCHed should be defined with
>>>> ODO.
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>The way I do it does exactly that.

Then you have do-it-yourself ODO.

>LARGE tables would NOT be searched serially, as I stated.
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>table... I use a single refmodded MOVE statement.

You are doing an insertion sort, a technique for sorting the table by shifting right as
new entries are inserted. The cost of your technique is, on average, n^2/4 if you search
for insertion points serially. Loading would be faster if you used a binary chop or
Shell's technique to find insertion points. It would be faster still if you left holes in
the table and shifted them out at the end. 

"Standard INSERTION SORT

In standard INSERTION SORT we maintain an array of elements in sorted order. When we
insert a new element, we find its target location and slide each element after this
location ahead by one array position to make room for the new insertion. The ith insertion
takes time O(i), for a total of O(n^2). Finding the target position of the ith element
takes time O(log i) using binary search, though this cost is dominated by the
insertion cost.

LIBRARY SORT

We achieve O(log n)-time insertions with high probability by keeping gaps evenly
distributed between the inserted elements and randomly permuting the input. Then we only
need to move a small number of elements ahead by one position until we reach a gap. The
more gaps we leave, the fewer elements we move on insertions. However, we can tolerate a
small-constant-space overhead."
http://tinyurl.com/3ajhm8

For example, the load time for 1,000 entries is 250,000 your way versus 6,908 this way.

>I therefore have no need whatsoever of ODO, it wouldn't have ANY advantage 
>over what I do now, and, as I keep telling you, it is UGLY! :-)

There is nothing ugly about remembering how many entries are in a table.
```

```
On Wed, 19 Mar 2008 12:44:35 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
>news:27hvt31hv14g0iah66usgm0b8lrmjpukj7@4ax.com...

>> You are doing an insertion sort, a technique for sorting the table by 
>> shifting right as
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>move to right shift the table.

I understood that, but it's a LOONG move. Every table element is being moved, on average,
n/4 places, which is why the load takes n^2 / 4 operations. Granted, being moved with a
LOONG move isn't as slow as being moved individually. 

Imagine a library clerk putting 1,000 books on a shelf by inserting each where it goes.
Your way has a single shelf that's 1,000 books long. Each time he inserts a book, he has
to shove to the right, on average, 250 books. That takes a lot of energy. 

Now picture 12 shelves each holding, on average, 84 books and having 16 empty spaces on
the right. Each insertion requires, on average, shifting 21 books to the right. Each
insertion takes 1/12 the energy (time). After insertions are done, the 11 gaps can be
closed by doing 11 LOONG (avg 500) shifts to the left. 

Your way moves 1,000 + (1,000 * 250) = 251,000 books. The librarian's way moves 1,000 +
(1,000 * 21) + (11 * 500) = 27,500 books, also using LOONG moves. 

The alternative is to run then through an external book sorter that runs in n(ln n) book
movements. That requires two extra passes: one to put them in and one to take them out.
Total book movements would be 9,908.
```

```
"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
news:55v0u39km08s5clelu7akv3ls5kpntku9b@4ax.com...
> On Wed, 19 Mar 2008 12:44:35 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" 
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> n/4 places, which is why the load takes n^2 / 4 operations.

ONE block of memory is being moved to another block of memory.

Yes, it is a long move, but that comes down to how storage to storage moves 
are implemented on the platform being used. And it is NOT my problem as a 
programmer to be concerned about that. Certainly a lot of bytes will be 
shuffled before the table is fully loaded, but that's what computers are 
for... and some platforms do it better than others.

While I understand your analogy of books on shelves, it is specious.

Four flaws, as follows:

1. Most computers have instruction sets that move bytes quickly. (So the 
"energy" (time) required to push books down the shelf is minimal)
2. You have not taken into account the overheads in setting up and utilising 
your "gaps".
3. Your "gapped" solution has 1200 entries instead of 1000 so it occupies 
more space.
4. It cannot be implemented with OCCURS DEPENDING ON so you couldn't use it 
anyway.

On reflection, I might agree it may run faster in theory, but it is not as 
easy to implement in a general form,  practically, as my solution is, and my 
solution is WAY better than OCCURS DEPENDING ON which is what this 
discussion is really all about.

You have failed to persuade me to change to ODO (but that was never likely 
to happen anyway... :-)

Pete.
```

- **From:** tim <TimJ@internet.com>
- **Date:** 2008-03-19T11:44:13
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<13u1v4dt59i28b6@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<1k6mt3hr864pk8co1j1orfm984j2lfqgvk@4ax.com> <18199eae-bffe-420c-8e32-cb6ebf7533c2@e25g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <k6sot397qpmqmiqqb1veampbojkv4gjb1t@4ax.com> <643ih8F2a3nu3U1@mid.individual.net> <6dmrt3dn0hueihi3p996d5n96sts0pgtm1@4ax.com> <6489stF2b6842U1@mid.individual.net> <6l7ut3h7qu4kt6bdkj7pl2ac0is7ut78ej@4ax.com> <64916eF2abdckU1@mid.individual.net> <27hvt31hv14g0iah66usgm0b8lrmjpukj7@4ax.com> <64b2f4F2aaa78U1@mid.individual.net> <55v0u39km08s5clelu7akv3ls5kpntku9b@4ax.com> <64cbi0F2aj7ckU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Thu, 20 Mar 2008 00:25:50 +1300, Pete Dashwood wrote:

> Four flaws, as follows:
> 
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

On the IBM mainframe COBOL the moves probably turn into multiple MVCs (max
256 bytes per instruction) or one MVCL. The MVCL is a restartable
instruction. In effect it gets split up into multiple instructions by the
microcode/hardware.

On X86 it likely ends up as rep movs or load/store words followed by loopX.

Above a fairly small size it is the total amount of data being moved that
dominates the time, not the number of instructions executed. The compiler
should be able to write code that is entirely constrained by memory
bandwidth. Some compilers will use the vector / SSE instructions to ensure
this is the case.

Whether you care how long it takes depends on how large the moves are and
how often they get executed. I have seen one program use several hours CPU
per day executing "move low-values to work-area". As your algorithm is
O(N**2) it will be slow for anything other than small tables.

Tim
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 24)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-03-20T14:12:20+13:00

```
with fetching the values (which takes many times longer), and the time is 
therefore three fifths of five eighths of fuck all (or three eighths of fuck 
all, if you want to be pedantic :-))
```

```
On Thu, 20 Mar 2008 00:25:50 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>
…[40 more quoted lines elided]…
>for... and some platforms do it better than others.

On the IBM z series, both MOVE CHARACTER (1 - 256 bytes) and MOVE
Character LONG (up to 16 million bytes as I recall) can be time
consuming, more so the latter since it is an interruptible instruction
and may have boundary alignment peculiarities.  This is on a system
that is oriented to moving data around.  I would look at ways to
minimize data movement.  Incidentally for tables where it is worth
having them in order and which may have a variable number of entries
depending on what is loaded at run time I would use an ODO and if my
input is sorted, I would use the limit field for the ODO as my
subscript.

Clark Morris
>
>While I understand your analogy of books on shelves, it is specious.
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>Pete.
```

```
On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 09:09:44 -0600, Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>On Mon, 17 Mar 2008 21:53:23 -0600, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>everybody else?   Was their thinking in this matter ever published?

Lots of muddled thinking on numeric errors has been published. Some talk about 'binary
arithmetic' without making a distinction between integer and floating point. Here's an
example:

" Why did computers use binary in the first place?

    Many early computers (such as the ENIAC, or the IBM 650) were in fact decimal
machines. In the 1950s, however, most computers turned to binary representations of
numbers as this made useful reductions in the complexity of arithmetic units (for example,
a binary adder requires about 15% less circuitry than a decimal adder). This reduction in
turn led to greater reliability and lower costs.

    Storing decimal integers in a simple binary coded decimal (BCD) form, rather than a
pure binary form, also uses up to 20% more storage than the binary form, depending on the
coding used.

    Decimal arithmetic, therefore, is inherently less efficient than binary arithmetic,
and at the time this justified the switch to binary floating-point arithmetic (just as a
two-digit representation for the year in a date was justifiable at the time). However, the
programming and conversion overheads and other costs of using binary arithmetic suggest
that hardware decimal arithmetic is now the more economical option for most applications. 

 Has any company formally announced hardware decimal floating-point support?

    Yes. IBM announced on 18 April 2007 hardware decimal floating point facilities for IBM
z9 EC and z9 BC: 

Since then, IBM has also announced support for decimal floating-point in the Power6
processors, and has released details of the decimal floating-point unit in the z6
microprocessor.
http://www2.hursley.ibm.com/decimal/decifaq3.html#calcs

Cowlishaw accidentally overlooks the fact that every Intel x86 processor/FPU made since
1980 has supported 17 digit hardware decimal floating point.  Now that IEEE 754r is on the
horizon, Intel is dropping it (I think they already did in Itanium).

"based on Intelï¿½s investigation on the frequency of
use and performance requirements for decimal processing in existing applications, the
potential benefits of hardware-implemented decimal arithmetic do not justify its cost in
terms of dedicating transistors and power consumption to it on a processor core.
Consequently, Intel is proposing the use of software emulation instead.

Just as the computing landscape changed dramatically from the ratification of the first
floating point standard to its current revision, equally big changes are in store for the
next 15 to 20 years. The multi-core processor revolution has begun and is poised to make
even greater leaps in performance (and performance per watt) for personal computers.
Intelï¿½s research and development plans suggest the possibility of a 256-core processor by
the 2015 time frame."
http://www.intel.com/standards/floatingpoint.pdf
```

```
On Mar 19, 2:34 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:


> Lots of muddled thinking on numeric ....

> Cowlishaw accidentally overlooks the fact that every Intel x86 processor/FPU made since
> 1980 has supported 17 digit hardware decimal floating point.

And none more muddled than yours, Robert.

No, it was the x86, it was the x87. In 1980 Intel was producing the
8086 and 8088. These did not provide what you claim, the 8087 did.
Even the much later 80286, 80386 and 80486SX did not support this as
they required an appropriate 80x87.

So your '_every_ Intel x86 .. since 1980' is wrong and simply more
misinformation.

The '17 digit hardware decimal floating point' is also wrong and is
misinformation.

The x87 does support 17 digit packed decimal _fixed_ point.

The x87 does support hardware floating point.

You have these muddled up.
```

- **References:** `<18199eae-bffe-420c-8e32-cb6ebf7533c2@e25g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <k6sot397qpmqmiqqb1veampbojkv4gjb1t@4ax.com> <643ih8F2a3nu3U1@mid.individual.net> <6dmrt3dn0hueihi3p996d5n96sts0pgtm1@4ax.com> <6489stF2b6842U1@mid.individual.net> <6l7ut3h7qu4kt6bdkj7pl2ac0is7ut78ej@4ax.com> <8kmvt3p72sq7j7o03b2bh2t0o813mjb2qn@4ax.com> <55k0u311ebnds0nib9t555ol6ejc2b2vjd@4ax.com> <cbbac620-9bb7-45b7-ac36-96536eced007@s37g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <42f1u31pasdqm2n7ji9kbtvvevm3ufpg2k@4ax.com> <aba5ad22-0eb1-40e3-9484-30472d78938d@m36g2000hse.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Wed, 19 Mar 2008 00:09:42 -0700, Richard wrote:

> On Mar 19, 8:14 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 20:32:26 -0700 (PDT), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>>

This is a bit academic for COBOL because

a) The packed decimal format supported is quite different from the
dominant standard ie IBM mainframe format.

b) The support for COBOL semantics is quite weak, in particular the
rounding, intermediate results. Other than packed load and store no other
packed operations are supported by the *87 unit. Everything else is done
as floating point with basically IEEE semantics.

c) The packed decimal operand is fixed length (18 digits). Shorter decimal
operands are not directly supported.

d) Validation is not performed on loads.

The *86 unit also supports some decimal instructions which are also of
limited value.
```

```
On Tue, 01 Apr 2008 12:44:49 GMT, "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com> wrote:

>"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
>news:jbd3v3tomdipsgke42j5cklujltahbtjle@4ax.com...
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>One-fifth is rational in decimal, but irrational in binary.

Nope, all three numbers are binary INTEGERS. The fallacy is in thinking of quotient as a
fraction. It is not a fraction, it is an integer.  The compiler SCALED two integers by
multiplying by powers of 10, then divided two binary integers to get a quotient with no
fraction. Binary 10 divided by binary 5 gives binary 2.

101111101011110000100000000 / 1100001101010000 = 11111010000

For ease of reading,  in base 16 and 10

5F5E100 / C350 = 7D0

100000000   / 50000  = 2000
```

```
Jeff Campbell wrote:

> DECDIV  Source Listing  3-APR-2008 06:13:52 -
> Compaq COBOL V2.8-1286            Page 1
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> Jeff

Feels like a bug in the compiler to me (I'll add it to our list). 
COMP-5 is mostly a synonym for COMP, but the compiler didn't honor that 
for this case.  If you change it to COMP you'll get an 8-byte binary 
integer for quotient.
```

```
IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
PROGRAM-ID. decdiv.
ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
DATA DIVISION.
WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
*

01      numerator             pic 9(9)         comp value 10.
01      denominator         pic 9(9)         comp value 5.
01      quotient                pic 9(9)v9(4) comp.

PROCEDURE DIVISION.

$ edit b.cob

IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
PROGRAM-ID. decdiv.
ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
DATA DIVISION.
WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.
01      numerator             pic 9(9)          comp value 10.
01      denominator         pic 9(9)          comp value 5.
01      quotient                pic 9(9)v9(4)  comp.

PROCEDURE DIVISION.
p0.
         compute quotient = numerator / denominator
*
* add display conversion
*
        display quotient with conversion

         stop run.

end program decdiv.

$ cobo/list decdiv
$ link decdiv
$ run decdiv
         2.0000
$
```

```
On Thu, 03 Apr 2008 20:15:01 -0300, Clark F Morris <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:

>I would be interested in the generated code for that operation.  I
>want to see if my belief that the code has to be complex is correct.

A 16-bit Realia (circa. 1984) did the operation in binary. I shortened the integers to 16
bits so we could see inline code. If they were 32 bits, we'd see only function calls. 

 01  numerator value 1 binary pic s9(4).
 01  denominator value 5 binary pic s9(4).
 01  quotient-integer binary pic s9(4).
 01  quotient-scaled binary pic s9(2)v9(2).

   divide numerator by denominator giving quotient-integer
   divide numerator by denominator giving quotient-scaled

   display quotient-integer
   display quotient-scaled
output:
0000
0020

code:
:0047 A1C600        MOV     AX,[00C6]                  
:004A 99                CWD
:004B F73EC800    IDIV    WORD PTR [00C8]
:004F A3CA00       MOV     [00CA],AX

:0052 BFF400        MOV     DI,00F4         ; temp area
:0055 BEC600        MOV     SI,00C6        ; numerator
:0058 BA0402        MOV     DX,0204
:005B B9FE20        MOV     CX,20FE
:005E 9A4F005918    CALL    1859:004F           ; scaling function
:0063 8B16F600    MOV     DX,[00F6]                ; 0
:0067 A1F400        MOV     AX,[00F4]                ; 64h = 100d
:006A F73EC800    IDIV    WORD PTR [00C8]   ; denominator
:006E A3CC00        MOV     [00CC],AX              ; quotient-scaled
```

```
On Sun, 16 Mar 2008 16:29:42 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>If each entry in a memory table is subject to an ODO, and the table itself 
>is also subject to ODO, then it seems pretty obvious that access will be 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>because the number of chops can be smaller on a sparsely populated table.

I agree.   And in CoBOL we also put in explicit tests
    IF MY-COUNT > COUNT-MAX
           DISPLAY "TABLE OVERFLOW ERROR IN MY-TABLE'
           GO TO ABORT-ROUTINE
   ELSE
          PEFORM ADD-ELEMENT-TO-TABLE
   END-IF.

Many languages make this more implicit (and I can add a compiler
switch to abend with table overflow).   I suppose we can do the test
several times, slowing it down.
```

- **References:** `<9c9f43b4-622c-431c-af76-f235c65015b0@s13g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <fk0ht3di28s4e20332rrgn566tb4fumel4@4ax.com> <f234e168-0774-46b9-afe7-9fb32376d5b6@i29g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <fdb338da-5ef5-4b95-af03-e834e9fe6df2@i12g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <72ijt3d13e2rvf0ha5u3b9fncvvr7m5h3e@4ax.com> <024ba9bd-51e9-4433-b34b-b541d02a9859@d4g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <1k6mt3hr864pk8co1j1orfm984j2lfqgvk@4ax.com> <18199eae-bffe-420c-8e32-cb6ebf7533c2@e25g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <k6sot397qpmqmiqqb1veampbojkv4gjb1t@4ax.com>`

```
On Sat, 15 Mar 2008 20:46:07 -0600, Robert wrote:

> Packed decimal arithmetic is slower than binary.
>    I did not doubt this, wanted to see how much slower. Results showed
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
This is undoubtedly true (on non-mainframe).

> Alignment on memory boundaries matters (generally on RISC machines)
>   True. I thought the L2 cache would make alignment a non-issue. I was
>   wrong. Alignment matters a lot more than I thought

I tested this on  a Pentium III and I had about 3% speed-up with
non-aligned data. The reason was that the L1/2/3 caches could fit more
data when it was non-aligned. Aligned data tends to take more space. So,
the value of alignment depends on the circumstances.


>>> The difference is not one or two chops (minimizing), it is a 10%
>>> reduction in search time.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Correct. Also you need to take into account the overhead of filling the
rest of the table with high-values or low-values. With occurs depending
you do not need to do this. I have seen this make a very big difference.

Also, I would not assume hashing is always the fastest method. Depending
on key size and complexity, the cost of calculating the hash value can be
relatively high. For small tables this can dominate the cost of the
search. Hash tables also take more space than an array.

Tim
```

- **References:** `<f234e168-0774-46b9-afe7-9fb32376d5b6@i29g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <fdb338da-5ef5-4b95-af03-e834e9fe6df2@i12g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <72ijt3d13e2rvf0ha5u3b9fncvvr7m5h3e@4ax.com> <024ba9bd-51e9-4433-b34b-b541d02a9859@d4g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <1k6mt3hr864pk8co1j1orfm984j2lfqgvk@4ax.com> <18199eae-bffe-420c-8e32-cb6ebf7533c2@e25g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <k6sot397qpmqmiqqb1veampbojkv4gjb1t@4ax.com> <13tp8h9qudnmn53@corp.supernews.com>`

```
On Sun, 16 Mar 2008 04:29:29 -0000, tim <TimJ@internet.com> wrote:

>Correct. Also you need to take into account the overhead of filling the
>rest of the table with high-values or low-values. With occurs depending
>you do not need to do this. I have seen this make a very big difference.

But that only needs to be done once, possibly during the compile. An
analysis of how it's going to be used will indicate whether this cost
will be paid for during subsequent transactions.

Although your compiler might check bounds whether or not the table is
variable length.   I don't know.

>Also, I would not assume hashing is always the fastest method. Depending
>on key size and complexity, the cost of calculating the hash value can be
>relatively high. For small tables this can dominate the cost of the
>search. Hash tables also take more space than an array.

When efficiency here is important, testing alternatives is called for.
But don't assume your test today applies to similar, but not identical
situation tomorrow.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2008-03-16T14:15:42+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<frja2e$chh$1@reader2.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<9c9f43b4-622c-431c-af76-f235c65015b0@s13g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <1k6mt3hr864pk8co1j1orfm984j2lfqgvk@4ax.com> <18199eae-bffe-420c-8e32-cb6ebf7533c2@e25g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <k6sot397qpmqmiqqb1veampbojkv4gjb1t@4ax.com>`

```
In article <k6sot397qpmqmiqqb1veampbojkv4gjb1t@4ax.com>,
Robert  <no@e.mail> wrote:

[snip]

>Add a third test that inserts the table into an indexed file and does
>the lookups with
>file reads. I predict it will be more than 10 times slower. 

I recall an application I was asked to look at, years on back... the 
company had bought out a customer-list and transaction volume had 
increased.  A program doing exactly this - read a record, grab a key 
field, use the key for an indexed READ - was moving into the Realm of 
Intolerable Slowness.

I implemented two changes:

1) sort the input file into ascending key-field sequence.

2) write code like...

IF WS-INREC-KEYFLD = WS-PREV-KEYFLD
    NEXT SENTENCE
ELSE
    PERFORM A84625-GET-KEYFLD-DESCRIPT  THRU  A84625-GKD-EX.

(oh... before folks get incensed about form over substance the compiler 
used was IKFCBL00 and the style above was reflective of shop-typical code)

Improved performance a fair bit, it did.

DD
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2008-03-16T11:58:28-07:00

```
>   They are actually slower.
>   This is a frequently asked question during technical interviews. Telling
>   them they're all wet will cause you to fail. You must lie better than the competition.
>

You simply do not notice that you continue to say blanket
generalisations. It is almost like you can only have binary logic.

Here is one response:

"""So it would appear on my box that an aligned index is fastest.  But
interestingly, simply defining an index slows you down if you intend

As it happens it is likely that your 'subscript was fastest' was
because the optimizer noticed the code was not actually doing anything
and optimised it away, which is unlikely in real code.


> Simple two-operand arithmetic statements are faster than COMPUTE.
>    Sometimes true, sometimes the same.

So two-operand are never slower. You set out to prove they were slower
and failed.

> The problem is caused by intermediate
>   results having needlessly high precision.

No it isn't 'needlessly'. Fujitsu (allegedly) does actually create
intermediate results that are the correct size, but this could be
slower than just having a large result.

> That's because compiler writers don't
>   grok number theory.

Another ad hominem against anonymous groups intended to denigrate them
compared to your superiority in all things.


> Do not use the REMAINDER, ROUNDED, ON SIZE ERROR or CORRESPONDING.
>   False for rounded; true for on size error.

No, it _was_ slower for ROUNDED but of course the result was
truncated.

> I didn't test remainder and corresponding
>  because it's obvious the advice is false.

'Obvious' in what way ?


> To execute a loop n times, set the counter to n and then decrement the counter until it
> becomes zero, rather than incrementing the counter from zero to n.
>   Turned out to be true. This is based on machine language instructons that loop until
>   a counter goes to zero. It is not a general truism because some machines have no
>   such instruction.

You completely missed the point on this one even after you had been
corrected several times.

And you _still_ don't understand.

The point was that when you have a limit variable with the number of
items used in a table then:

   PERFORM VARYING i FROM 1 BY 1 UNTIL 1 > mylimit

is comparing to a _data_item_ each iteration, while:

   PERFORM VARYING i FROM mylimit BY -1 UNTIL 1 = ZERO

is comparing to a _literal_. It has nothing to do with whether the
machine instruction does a zero compare.

Your 'tests' used a literal of the number of occurs in the table
instead of the mylimit data item.

> Packed decimal arithmetic is slower than binary.
>    I did not doubt this, wanted to see how much slower. Results showed packed is about
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>   matters a lot more than I thought

So, in summary, you never _actually_ 'busted' any of these.
```

```
 identification division.

* ODO 5 items            1361

* ODO search            11801   9-


 data division.
 working-storage section.
 01  test-data.
     05  s-destination     binary          pic s9(09).
     05  DO                binary          pic s9(09).
     05  s-subscript       binary          pic s9(09).
     05  search-subject    binary          pic s9(09).

 01  depending-area.
     05  depending-entry occurs 1 to 1024 depending on DO
            indexed x-depending ascending key depending-item-1.
         10 depending-item-1  binary       pic s9(09).
         10 depending-item-2  binary       pic s9(09).
         10 depending-item-3  binary       pic s9(09).
         10 depending-item-4  binary       pic s9(09).
         10 depending-item-5  binary       pic s9(09).
 01  fixed-area.
     05  fixed-entry occurs 1024
           indexed x-fixed ascending key fixed-item-1.
         10 fixed-item-1  binary       pic s9(09).
         10 fixed-item-2  binary       pic s9(09).
         10 fixed-item-3  binary       pic s9(09).
         10 fixed-item-4  binary       pic s9(09).
         10 fixed-item-5  binary       pic s9(09).
 01  timer-variables.
     05  test-name                         pic  x(30).
     05  repeat-factor value 100000000 binary pic s9(09).
     05  current-date-structure.
         10                                pic  x(08).
         10  time-now-hhmmsshh.
             15  hours                         pic  9(02).
             15  minutes                       pic  9(02).
             15  seconds                       pic  9(02).
             15  hundredths                    pic  9(02).
         10                                pic  x(05).
     05  time-now                          pic  9(06)v99.
     05  time-start                        pic  9(06)v99.
     05  timer-overhead value zero         pic  9(06)v99.
     05  elapsed-time                      pic s9(06)v99.
     05  comparison-time                   pic  9(06)v99.
     05  elapsed-time-display.
         10  elapsed-time-edited           pic  z(05).
         10  comparison-percentage         pic  z(04)-.

  procedure division.

     initialize test-data
     perform varying s-subscript from 1024 by -1
         until s-subscript = zero
         move s-subscript to depending-item-1 (s-subscript)
                             depending-item-2 (s-subscript)
                             depending-item-3 (s-subscript)
                             depending-item-4 (s-subscript)
                             depending-item-5 (s-subscript)
         move s-subscript to fixed-item-1 (s-subscript)
                             fixed-item-2 (s-subscript)
                             fixed-item-3 (s-subscript)
                             fixed-item-4 (s-subscript)
                             fixed-item-5 (s-subscript)
     end-perform
     move 10 to s-subscript
     move 500 to DO
     move zero to comparison-time

     move 'Timer overhead' to test-name
     perform timer-on
     perform timer-on
     perform repeat-factor times
         continue
     end-perform
     perform timer-off
     compute timer-overhead = (time-now - time-start)

      multiply 5 by repeat-factor

     move 'Fixed 1 item' to test-name
     perform timer-on
     perform repeat-factor times
         move fixed-item-1 (s-subscript) to s-destination
     end-perform
     perform timer-off
     move elapsed-time to comparison-time

     move 'ODO 1 item' to test-name
     perform timer-on
     perform repeat-factor times
         move depending-item-1 (s-subscript) to s-destination
     end-perform
     perform timer-off

     divide 10 into repeat-factor

     move 'Fixed 5 items' to test-name
     perform timer-on
     perform repeat-factor times
         move fixed-item-1 (s-subscript) to s-destination
         move fixed-item-2 (s-subscript) to s-destination
         move fixed-item-3 (s-subscript) to s-destination
         move fixed-item-4 (s-subscript) to s-destination
         move fixed-item-5 (s-subscript) to s-destination
     end-perform
     perform timer-off
     move elapsed-time to comparison-time

     move 'ODO 5 items' to test-name
     perform timer-on
     perform repeat-factor times
         move depending-item-1 (s-subscript) to s-destination
         move depending-item-2 (s-subscript) to s-destination
         move depending-item-3 (s-subscript) to s-destination
         move depending-item-4 (s-subscript) to s-destination
         move depending-item-5 (s-subscript) to s-destination
     end-perform
     perform timer-off

     divide 2 into repeat-factor

     move 'Fixed search' to test-name
     perform timer-on
     move zero to search-subject
     perform repeat-factor times
         add 1 to search-subject
         if  search-subject greater than 500
             move 1 to search-subject
         end-if
         search all fixed-entry
            at end display 'Test failed'
            when fixed-item-1 (x-fixed) = search-subject
               move zero to s-destination
         end-search
     end-perform
     perform timer-off
     move elapsed-time to comparison-time

     move 'ODO search' to test-name
     perform timer-on
     move zero to search-subject
     perform repeat-factor times
         add 1 to search-subject
         if  search-subject greater than 500
             move 1 to search-subject
         end-if
         search all depending-entry
            at end display 'Test failed'
            when depending-item-1 (x-depending) = search-subject
               move zero to s-destination
         end-search
     end-perform
     perform timer-off

     exit program

 . timer-on.
     perform read-the-time
     move time-now to time-start
 . timer-off.
     perform read-the-time
     compute elapsed-time rounded = ((time-now - time-start)
         * 100000000 / repeat-factor)
        - timer-overhead

     move spaces to elapsed-time-display
     if  elapsed-time not greater than zero
         display elapsed-time space time-start space time-now
         move 'error' to elapsed-time-display
     else
         compute elapsed-time-edited rounded = elapsed-time * 100
         if  comparison-time not equal to zero
             compute comparison-percentage rounded = 100 *
                ((elapsed-time / comparison-time) - 1)
             move zero to comparison-time
         end-if
     end-if
     display test-name elapsed-time-display
 . read-the-time.
     accept time-now-hhmmsshh from time
     compute time-now =
        ((((hours * 60) +
           minutes) * 60) +
           seconds) +
           (hundredths / 100)
```
