>> >> >> It is easy to picture that happening, because the optimizer thinks the 1st or 2nd move
>> >> >> might have changed either the subscript or the DO item, so it recomputes the subscript for
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>This is where the self styled 'theoritician' fails.

I know what machine language is required. What I don't know is which features will
generate function calls that slow execution. I can guess, based on the knowledge that
compiler companies optimize features used most frequently in the real world.

>> It gets the size from the DO item and multiplies by the reach of an occurrance.
>> >> That's two instructions. The alternative is to load the length from a literal. Time for
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>correct code.

Compilation and optimization are done in at least two stages. A front end translates
source to an intermediate language; a back end translates intermediate to machine
language. There is a separate back end for each CPU/OS. That's evident in Java and .NET,
where the back end is written by another company. You can see it in Micro Focus, which
produces a visible .int(ermediate) file. It is not as visible in the GNU Compiler
Collection (gcc), but they all share the same back ends.

Thus, the compiler (front end) writer doesn't always know what the back end will do with
the intermediate code he generates. 

>> They struck me as folklore, maxims widely believed in
>> the Cobol community, but simply not true.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>generation by listening to gossip around the water cooler ?

When advice sounds like mainframe 'wisdom', it makes me think the writer is an old-timer,
not a hands-on compiler developer. 

>> I picked the ones that sounded wrong and tested
>> them. Results showed Micro Focus' advice was wrong in some cases. Specifically:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>generalisations. It is almost like you can only have binary logic.

Local programming standards are ALL generalizations. I don't see you arguing for their
abolition. 

>Here is one response:
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>and optimised it away, which is unlikely in real code.

In that case, execution time would have been zero.

>> Simple two-operand arithmetic statements are faster than COMPUTE.
>>    Sometimes true, sometimes the same.
>
>So two-operand are never slower. You set out to prove they were slower
>and failed.

I set out to prove there is no reason to avoid COMPUTE. 

>> The problem is caused by intermediate
>>   results having needlessly high precision.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>slower than just having a large result.

I think the problem will go away when we get decimal arithmetic in the FPU.  IEEE 754r
should be approved soon. Earlier attempts in IEEE 854 and ISO/IEC 10967 were not adopted
by chip makers. 

>> That's because compiler writers don't
>>   grok number theory.
>
>Another ad hominem against anonymous groups intended to denigrate them
>compared to your superiority in all things.

Tell Prof. Kahan. 

"Perhaps the only way to inhibit floating-point pejorations in the guise of
ï¿½optimizationsï¿½ is via education of the programming language community and its clientele."
http://www.cs.berkeley.edu/~wkahan/Mindless.pdf

>> Do not use the REMAINDER, ROUNDED, ON SIZE ERROR or CORRESPONDING.
>>   False for rounded; true for on size error.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>'Obvious' in what way ?

There is no reason to think ADD CORRESPONDING would be slower than individual ADDs. Every
machine I've worked on puts the remainder in a register. Moreover, alternative ways of
getting a remainder would surely be slower.

>> To execute a loop n times, set the counter to n and then decrement the counter until it
>> becomes zero, rather than incrementing the counter from zero to n.
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>instead of the mylimit data item.

I changed it after you pointed that out, which is why I said "turned out to be". 
It shouldn't make any difference. If you're not changing the limit inside the loop, the
optimizer should load it once and hold it in a register. 

>> Packed decimal arithmetic is slower than binary.
>>    I did not doubt this, wanted to see how much slower. Results showed packed is about
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>So, in summary, you never _actually_ 'busted' any of these.

I busted your 8% claim for ODO.

> predictions. Here's the program:

> >You simply do not notice that you continue to say blanket
> >generalisations. It is almost like you can only have binary logic.
>
> Local programming standards are ALL generalizations. I don't see you arguing for their
> abolition.

Why would I care whether Local programming standards are abolished ?

> >As it happens it is likely that your 'subscript was fastest' was
> >because the optimizer noticed the code was not actually doing anything
> >and optimised it away, which is unlikely in real code.
>
> In that case, execution time would have been zero.

Not necessarily, the PERFORM loop may have run through but the code
inside did not change anything so was only done once.


> >> Simple two-operand arithmetic statements are faster than COMPUTE.
> >>    Sometimes true, sometimes the same.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> I set out to prove there is no reason to avoid COMPUTE.

and failed.


> >> The problem is caused by intermediate
> >>   results having needlessly high precision.
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> "optimizations" is via education of the programming language community and its clientele."http://www.cs.berkeley.edu/~wkahan/Mindless.pdf

> >> Do not use the REMAINDER, ROUNDED, ON SIZE ERROR or CORRESPONDING.
> >>   False for rounded; true for on size error.
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
> optimizer should load it once and hold it in a register.

How does it know you are not changing the limit inside the loop ? It
could be done by a side effect somewhere.


> >> Packed decimal arithmetic is slower than binary.
> >>    I did not doubt this, wanted to see how much slower. Results showed packed is about
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> I busted your 8% claim for ODO.

No. Wrong. This is another of your 'a single measure makes a universal
truth'.

You showed (possibly) that the Wintel version that you used for the
program that you wrote did not exhibit the same performance that the
compiler that I used did.

However, when I compiled and ran your program I got some timing of
'error' which implied that, once again, you had fallen into the trap
of having test code that can be optimised away. You had used a fixed
subscript in a perform loop, so naturally the optimiser on the
compiler that I use removed the access from inside the loop and did it
once.

So I changed the looping to make it a nested PERFORM n TIMES (with n
1000 times smaller) and inside this a PERFORM VARYING s-subscript FROM
1024 BY -1 UNTIL ..

This munted the overhead calculation but did give a 4% slower access
on 'ODO 5 items' compared to 'fixed 5 items' now that the looping does
actually do some accesses.

The 4% is comparable to my original 1% with single access and 8% with
9 accesses per loop.

The SEARCH is 4% faster, if the number of chops is reduced by 10% then
the reason this is not achieved (on the compiler that I use) is likely
to be less efficient access.
```

On Sun, 16 Mar 2008 20:38:27 -0600, Robert <no@e.mail> wrote:

>When advice sounds like mainframe 'wisdom', it makes me think the writer is an old-timer,
>not a hands-on compiler developer. 

And when advice sounds like PC 'wisdom', it makes me think the writer
is a new-timer, not a hands-on compiler developer.

Old timers were much more concerned with pure run-time efficiency than
new-timers, who are more concerned with programming efficiency.
```

```
On Thu, 13 Mar 2008 12:41:56 -0700 (PDT), Richard <riplin@azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Mar 13, 2:44 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
>> On Wed, 12 Mar 2008 11:30:03 -0700 (PDT), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
>NO. They said _ACCESS_ was inefficient.

ACCESS describes everything you can do with data. 

>> When I presented evidence that
>> ODO is not slow, they criticized my test as inadequate because it conflicted with their
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>consistently 1% slower for simple MOVEs.

I posted a timing test of simple MOVEs. That test did not measure speed IMPROVEMENTS due
to ODO, such as moving fewer bytes. 

>ODO was used primarily for data records where the actual record size
>is controlled by the DO. What testing did you do with this ?  Perhaps
>it is 'inefficient' to MOVE records between W-S and FD record areas
>when there is an ODO.

I haven't used files in 20 years (except line sequential). When I did use files, I very
seldom had ODO items in them. Thus, I never thought of ODO as a file oriented feature. I
use ODO for tables and dynamically allocated list entries containing variable length
strings.

>They didn't say it made SEARCH ALL 'inefficient'.

They didn't say that. They said avoid ODO in general.

>> You can't have it both ways.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>'right'.

Many are cold, but few are frozen.

>ODO used for file systems, record areas, and associated W-S moves may
>well be inefficient, and also may well be a bad thing suitable for
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>While it _may_ make SEARCH ALL save an access or two, 

Or several million per second. When you're processing 15,000 transactions per second --
yes, with Cobol -- speed matters. If Cobol can't keep up, it will be replaced by another
language (C++) that can. The other language is not inherently faster; the difference is a
programming culture that doesn't find variable length tables 'too complicated'.

>if the
>consequence is that _all_ other accesses are slower then it is not
>useful.
>
>In any case SEARCH ALL seems not to be widely used.

Many Cobol features are not widely used. 'If we didn't have it in The Good Old Days, we
don't need it.' That resistance to change is why Cobol is dying. That's why Object Cobol
was a flop and the 2002 standard is not being implemented. 

>> >> >Whether it makes the search faster or slower is dependent on the
>> >> >implementation, as well as many other things.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>written in order to make this sweeping claim.

Binary searches and compilers are written by systems programmers, not applications
programmers. Companies with poorly written compilers went out of business.  

>But, in any case, you have _completely_ missed the point. 'access' is
>done when the program puts each data item into the table, or retrieves
>it, or the SEARCH  compares the keys in the table entry against the
>required value.

True. What IS the point?

>> >In any case, even if the SEARCH ALL is faster it may be that loading
>> >the table is slower, especially if the DO is incremented with each
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>a different instruction sequence somewhere.

One percent isn't much. You didn't measure the improvements.

>Now logically I should change the DO every time I add an item to the
>table and this, in itself, could make it slower.

I doubt it will make any difference. The only way it could is if the optimizer is holding
the DO in a register. 

>Of course the real overhead of using ODO comes when there are multiple
>or nested ODOs and then access is really complicated, and very
>inefficient.

Shades of PL/I. I didn't know that was possible until I read it here recently. 

>Also Micro Focus may be your whole world, but others use different
>compilers which may well have different performance characteristics.

Comparative timings posted here don't show wide differences in compiler efficiency.
Differences are usually caused by hardware such as packed arithmetic, and software
components such as OS and file system.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net>
- **Date:** 2008-03-14T12:44:05-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ZQyCj.30468$yk5.26215@newsfe18.lga>`
- **References:** `<47D56BE4.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <m1pbt3hf9l197putsqlr2pvr1iccpgv7ka@4ax.com> <dDzBj.440419$Ib6.70764@fe03.news.easynews.com> <f6eet3depq6ugth6br7r2mk1hd5u9uiiqk@4ax.com> <1fb17dec-e2a3-4232-82e4-6b871fee7cb1@s12g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <oaift3hdet7beqoji4jeqfgtr64sbi1bk4@4ax.com> <9c9f43b4-622c-431c-af76-f235c65015b0@s13g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <fk0ht3di28s4e20332rrgn566tb4fumel4@4ax.com> <f234e168-0774-46b9-afe7-9fb32376d5b6@i29g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <5e0kt39okdd6e0osvmaqsmul452fvqathk@4ax.com>`

```
Referring strictly to the question of "occurs depending on" and allocation
of memory for it: seems to me it's a non-issue.  With the availability of
virtual memory, there is obviously no problem with allocating the maximum
array size: all that's actually needed in the compiled program is a pointer
of some sort to the virtual memory segment that it's stored in.  Dynamically
expanding or contracting an array with vm is a pretty problem in
under-the-cover programming but nothing more.  The virtual storage manager
looks after all the messy details and the "depending on" dataname specifies
the "actually used" number of entries.  I dare say the number of pointers to
vm segments is unlimited since they also can be stored in a vm segment.

Of course there are performance issues here but on the other hand truly
gigantic physical memory spaces are no longer news.

Unless I've missed something crucial in the discussion - only those
compilers that actually allocate space as part of the compiled unit will run
into problems with this.

PL
```

```
On Fri, 14 Mar 2008 12:44:05 -0600, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

>Referring strictly to the question of "occurs depending on" and allocation
>of memory for it: seems to me it's a non-issue.  With the availability of
>virtual memory, there is obviously no problem with allocating the maximum
>array size: all that's actually needed in the compiled program is a pointer
>of some sort to the virtual memory segment that it's stored in. 

You talk like a Computer Scientist. 

> Dynamically
>expanding or contracting an array with vm is a pretty problem in
>under-the-cover programming but nothing more.  

There is MUCH more involved when the address of the array can change. 

Cobol ODOs are not dynamically re-allocatable. The standard says their address is static.

>The virtual storage manager
>looks after all the messy details and the "depending on" dataname specifies
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>gigantic physical memory spaces are no longer news.

There are HUGE performance issues with the kind of abstractions you're talking about.
Today I opened a Java page that took TWO MINUTES to load. When it tried to use its Send
Email function, it crapped out and provided a stack trace that was at least 100 entries
deep. 

I'm a techie and I couldn't figure out what the problem was. A typical dumb user wouldn't
stand a chance. 

>Unless I've missed something crucial in the discussion - only those
>compilers that actually allocate space as part of the compiled unit will run
>into problems with this.

No compiler does that today. Its output program could become a shared dll/so thing with a
copy of working-storage for each user process.

But ODOs *are* statically allocated within that data space. They are not dynamic.
```

> stand a chance.

Pray tell - what does this anecdote have to do with varying-size arrays and
vm?

>
> >Unless I've missed something crucial in the discussion - only those
> >compilers that actually allocate space as part of the compiled unit will
run
> >into problems with this.
>
> No compiler does that today. Its output program could become a shared
dll/so thing with a
> copy of working-storage for each user process.
>
> But ODOs *are* statically allocated within that data space. They are not
dynamic.

The compiled program does not have to know anything about where the array is
actually stored in memory, real or virtual.  And even if the space is
allocated statically, in one maximum-sized glump, the vm manager will smear
it out all over the paging devices.  I'm talking operating system functions,
not prorgam-controlled functions.  Can you say "memory leak?"

PL
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>
- **Date:** 2008-03-16T13:59:43-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0ruqt3990q6le5tm31ilk8ti1c157et5e0@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<1fb17dec-e2a3-4232-82e4-6b871fee7cb1@s12g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <oaift3hdet7beqoji4jeqfgtr64sbi1bk4@4ax.com> <9c9f43b4-622c-431c-af76-f235c65015b0@s13g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <fk0ht3di28s4e20332rrgn566tb4fumel4@4ax.com> <f234e168-0774-46b9-afe7-9fb32376d5b6@i29g2000prf.googlegroups.com> <5e0kt39okdd6e0osvmaqsmul452fvqathk@4ax.com> <ZQyCj.30468$yk5.26215@newsfe18.lga> <4bbmt3hscg12map55darfj7sqftcdtnhk0@4ax.com> <FBXCj.74337$Ft5.68994@newsfe15.lga>`

```
On Sat, 15 Mar 2008 16:54:24 -0600, "tlmfru" <lacey@mts.net> wrote:

>
>Robert <no@e.mail> wrote in message
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>Thank you.  I think.  Or are you saying that comnputer scientists are wrong?

They are dismissive of old technology, where old is defined as 'before my time'.

>> > Dynamically
>> >expanding or contracting an array with vm is a pretty problem in
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>Maybe so.  But it's all handled by the vm manager/paging subsytem.

Sure. Just drop a symbolic link at the old address, pointing to the new address.

>> Cobol ODOs are not dynamically re-allocatable. The standard says their
>address is static.
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
>vm?

It was a general swipe at excessive abstraction.

>> >Unless I've missed something crucial in the discussion - only those
>> >compilers that actually allocate space as part of the compiled unit will
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>not prorgam-controlled functions.  Can you say "memory leak?"

Can you say "pointer?" A pointer IS awareness of where the thing is stored in memory. When
a program calls another passing the structure, it doesn't pass the structure's name, it
passes the address. If the called program causes that address to change, there must be a
way to communicate the new address back to the caller. 

You talk as though there is a layer of indirection between the program and memory, a layer
that translates 'handles' into memory addresses. Yes, there is such a layer in hardware,
but for backward compatibility the handles are made to look like a linear address space. 
The reason is so programs can do arithmetic on them. If you allow arrays, the implication
is that the location of element n can be computed. If each element has a unique handle
then you have an array of handles. How do you find handle n if not by arithmetic? A string
is an array of bytes. How do you propose finding byte 2 if not by adding 1 to the address
of byte 1?

A byte is an association of bits. In a world of perfect abstraction, each bit would have a
handle. Then there would be only two bits of real data in memory: a 0 and a 1. Everything
else in the computer would be associations of handles to those two bits.
```

```


"Alistair" <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote in message 
news:80b62260-98a3-4aaa-a674-e7266594ff38@i29g2000prf.googlegroups.com...
> On 12 Mar, 03:38, Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>> > >"Robert" <n...@e.mail> wrote in message
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> Yes they can.

Oh, no they can't...   (Look out behind you...!!!)

As an essential part of Relational Database Normalization (simplification, 
if you like...) "repeating groups" (arrays, represented in COBOL by use of 
the OCCURS clause) MUST be removed. This is a requirement of Second Normal 
form (2NF).

Rows on a Relational Database therefore cannot contain arrays, in the sense 
that COBOL people understand an array.

I suppose, on an unormalized DB you COULD allocate a fixed number of columns 
and let each one contain an array entry, but you couldn't reference them 
with subscripts and you would have to load all of those columns into a 
memory array before you could. Obviously, this would be such a horror show 
that no-one in their right mind would consider it.

Robert's statement is fair, and represents what you would encounter on a 
properly normalized RDB.

Pete.
```

On Sat, 15 Mar 2008 13:40:12 -0700 (PDT), Alistair <alistair@ld50macca.demon.co.uk> wrote:

>On 15 Mar, 19:39, Howard Brazee <how...@brazee.net> wrote:
>> On Fri, 14 Mar 2008 12:11:18 -0700 (PDT), Alistair

>Although it took me two reads (above average) to get your drift.

Golf just looks boring. It is actually a supreme test of athletic ability.

When I was a caddy I sometimes got country club guests who knew nothing about golf.
Ordinary civilians did terrible on their first round; they shot like 140. The ones who
were college football stars (natural athletes) were different. They would study people
teeing off on the the first tee, and accurately copy their swing. They instinctively 'got
it'.  With a little more tutelage from me, they'd break 100 on their first round. That's
pretty remarkable, because there are no-talent regular golfers who can't break 100 after
playing for years. 

I had a friend who was a college basketball star. Within a month after taking up golf he
was shooting par. I introduced him to racquetball, another test of athletic ability that
outsiders mistakenly think is a pitty-pat sport. Within a month he was beating me. He went
on to win open class (the highest category) tournaments.
```

…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>The green is 150 yards away, what club should he use?

He should use a 6. 

I caddied for four years as a teenager. Some of my clients were money players or golf
hustlers. I remember a game when we got to the 18th tee it was even, and a thunderstorm
started. Other players and caddies, believing the myth that clubs attract lightning, were
cowering for cover. My guy reached into his bag, which weighed 70 pounds (I weighed it
later in the pro shop), and pulled out a complete Goretex rain suit. Also gloves designed
specifically for rain. 

Others drove timorously, trying to get it over as quickly as possible. Their balls were
short and/or in the rough. My guy had an excellent drive, in the middle of the fairway and
160 yards from the green. As we approached the ball, he asked, "What do you think? Is a 6
too much?" I thought to myself, "Jesus Christ, you're hitting into a 20 mph headwind rain.
I'd use a  5." What I actually said was, "No it's not too much, use a 6." He took my
advice. His ball landed on the green but rolled off the back. As we walked down the
fairway he muttered to someone "I knew I shouldn't have listened to the goddamed caddy."

He scored par and won several hundred dollars from the others, who all got bogies. But I
vividly remember that moment, when I was unfairly criticized for being right.
```

Two Zen Buddhist monks were walking down a path and reached the bank of a 
rain-swollen river.  Standing on the bank was a pretty young woman who 
asked if they could help carry her across.

The young monk refused.  The old monk asked if the young monk would carry 
his pack across the river; when the young monk agreed the old monk told 
the woman to climb on his back and he carried her to the other side.

That evening the young monk confronted the old monk.  'What you did back 
there, Brother, was dangerous.  We are monks, we are not to have dealings 
with women - especially young, pretty ones! - for that path leads to 
temptation and disgrace.'

The old monk replied 'You are still carrying that woman?  I put her down 
as soon as we crossed the river.'

It has been a few decades since you were a teenager, Mr Wagner; perhaps 
the Statute of Limitations for this particular incident has expired.

memory, then goes and allocates the largest possible space anyway. THAT'S 
why I REALLY don't like it... it simply fails to deliver what it promises. 
:-) (Failure to deliver, usually pisses me off... :-))

Now, your point about sorts on ODO arrays being faster is an interesting 
one, but, as I have never used an ODO array in memory and therefore have no

beneath contempt as far as I'm concerned... In a bygone era, when external 
media were limited in capacity and expensive in price, there MIGHT have been 
a need for it (although even then we found other and better ways to store 
information), but anyone still using it today for external storage just gets 
my sympathy and an offer of help. No discussion.)

Bill obviously lives in a different world from me. If I was living in a 
world where there were a "LARGE number of files that are still used by COBOL 
programs and that require ODO's..." I'd be strongly encouraging migration to 
databases... By an amazing coincidence, I just happen to have here some 
tools that can fully automate the data migration and also some approaches 
that can help with the code conversion. Am I prophetic or what? (Actually, 
no... it's just that quite a number of people have realized that living in a 
world where there are a "LARGE number of files that are still used by COBOL 
programs and that require ODO's..." is NOT a good place to be...) I was one 
of them, without even having the increased urgency that ODO would provide, 
so I built some tools to help me get out of this dying world. Some other 
people then also picked them up... I thought their usefulness was now over 
as, surely, everybody has migrated?  Apparently not... at least not where 
Bill lives :-).

No they don't. But they can vary in length, WITHOUT needing ODO... VARCHAR 
columns are implemented the way ODO SHOULD have been... transparently to the 
user.

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-03-12T07:30:42+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<CELBj.64272$I5.51755@fe09.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<HAXAj.5290$FE.820@fe05.news.easynews.com> <47D56BE4.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <m1pbt3hf9l197putsqlr2pvr1iccpgv7ka@4ax.com> <dDzBj.440419$Ib6.70764@fe03.news.easynews.com> <f6eet3depq6ugth6br7r2mk1hd5u9uiiqk@4ax.com> <63p2sfF28n6tgU1@mid.individual.net> <13teonpobo7kve4@corp.supernews.com> <63p7ugF27vleuU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Actually, I have used MAX, MIN, ORD-MAX, ORD-MIN with an ALL subscript and a 
fixed number of occurrence table.  The "trick" is to fill with high-values or 
low-values before populating the table.  for example pre-fill with low-values 
and then use
  Function Max (elem (all)) ...

and it will happily find the maximum entry.

SUM could work if you initialize to zero.

I don't know how "performance" OR "maintainability" would work when compared to 
using an ODO for the same logic (and not having those "extra" table entries).

As far as Pete's question, the date, numval and the min/max, ord/char functions 
are the ones that I think have been MOST commonly used (at least in IBM 
mainframe and Micro Focus applications that I know).  All of these were from the 
'89 Intrinsic functions amendment and not added by the '02 Standard.  (OK, 
random also gets used - but it always surprises people how it actually works - 
and I have  also seen REM/MOD in more than one application.)  I guess I have 
seen everything except the statistics and trig functions <G>.

The two functions that I "like" from the '02 Standard - that I don't think are 
used much (and I am not certain who has implemented them) are:
  highest-algebraic
    and
  lowest-algebraic

As someone who (back in my paid programming days) occasionally fell into the 
"trap" moving zeroes or 9's to a numeric field and NOT getting its 
highest/lowest value, I think these were really "nice to have" new features.
```

length can be calculated at compile time, for fixed references. I would 
agree there probably isn't much to choose between variable references, but 
I'd still have to code that awful ODO and it is UGLY!!!! :-)

>> news:38kft3t3h52mm32b1vekgqe1qu98l7l1la@4ax.com...
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>some vendors differed, for example, for 01-levels and 77-levels.

8.6.3 of the 2002 standard

Data items and file connectors defined in the working-storage, communication, or file
section of a source element that is not an initial program are static items. Also, screen
item attributes in a source element that is not an initial program are treated as static
items. These items are set to their initial state each time the runtime element or object
containing them is set to its initial state, as described in 14.5.2.1.2.1, Initial state,
and in 14.5.2.2, Initial state of object data. They are allocated no later than
immediately before initialization and persist to the first of the
following:
ï¿½ the end of the run unit,

A based entry is a data description entry in the working-storage section, local-storage
section, or linkage section that is described with a BASED clause. A based entry is not
initially associated with an actual data item. An association is established linking the
based entry to actual data when its implicit data-address pointer is assigned the address
of an existing data item or assigned the address of storage obtained with an ALLOCATE
statement. This association establishes a based data item.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: RW on Working-Storage (was: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-03-13T06:28:30+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<iQ3Cj.36812$FE.5240@fe05.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<HAXAj.5290$FE.820@fe05.news.easynews.com> <47D56BE4.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <m1pbt3hf9l197putsqlr2pvr1iccpgv7ka@4ax.com> <dDzBj.440419$Ib6.70764@fe03.news.easynews.com> <f6eet3depq6ugth6br7r2mk1hd5u9uiiqk@4ax.com> <63p2sfF28n6tgU1@mid.individual.net> <38kft3t3h52mm32b1vekgqe1qu98l7l1la@4ax.com> <63q361F28e93nU1@mid.individual.net> <3NQBj.69308$3y2.47103@fe08.news.easynews.com> <lr3ht3pi8lejvamj0qjgpfdbdqo88336s6@4ax.com>`

```
I can certainly understand the confussion but calling something "static data" 
(and defining what that means does NOT mean that the storage is allocated 
statically (which was your statement).  The section that you quoted from is 
under

  "8.6.3 Automatic, initial, and static internal items"

The first paragraph explains what all of this is about, i.e.

"Each internal item has one of the three persistence attributes: automatic, 
initial, or static. The designation of automatic, initial, and static items 
relates to their persistence and the persistence of their contents during the 
execution of the run unit."

Notice inparticular that for "automatic" data, it says,
  "Their storage is allocated and set to initial state each time the runtime 
element containing them is activated."

For "static" items, the statement is quite different.  It says,

"They are allocated no later than immediately before initialization"

Which I think (to me) is NOT what you said, i.e.

" The standard says working-storage is allocated statically."
```

On Thu, 13 Mar 2008 06:28:30 GMT, "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>I can certainly understand the confussion but calling something "static data"

The problem with abstract sounding language is that peoples' eyes glaze over and they
forget the original question. Let's try to stay focussed on one question -- plain vanilla
working-storage. Of the three choices below, it is the one called static. Forget automatic
and initial, concentrate on what the standard says about static. 

>The section that you quoted from is 
>under
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>" The standard says working-storage is allocated statically."

Initialization means at program load time. Statically also means at program load time. In
plain English, the standard says 'working-storage is allocated at program load time.'  I
said "working-storage is allocated at program load time." There is no confusion.

NO, Robert, that is simply the same _INTERPRETATION_ of what you think

When you make a mistake you merely repeat it until everyone gives up.
(and then later claim that you were right anyway).

If my program, loaded and running, CALLs a subroutine for the first
then it will create a new working-storage for that. When I CANCEL it
will destroy that area and make it available for reuse by another.

This could happen even if the program was linked so that the program
code loaded with the run unit.

In OO COBOL a new object will dynamically create a new working-storage
for itself.

```
On Mar 14, 6:37 pm, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
> On Thu, 13 Mar 2008 11:11:08 -0700 (PDT), Richard <rip...@azonic.co.nz> wrote:
> >On Mar 14, 3:14 am, Robert <n...@e.mail> wrote:
…[63 more quoted lines elided]…
> the standard. The CANCELled program was NOT reinitialized. Now you are claiming it is.

No, I am claiming that it _could_, just as my words _actually_ say.

If the dll or .so is linked so that it loads at the same time as the
run-unit then the W-S area for the programs that are in the dll or .so
don't need to be created until the program is first CALLed. This is
because the W-S is part of the run-unit but the code is part of
the .so.

That is, the initialisation is only required at the time when the
program in the library is CALLed for the first time. It is at this
point that the W-S is allocated and initialised. There may be several
or dozens of programs in a dll or .so, only a few of them actually
being called in a particular run. As the .so is _shared_ with other
run-units the W-S for those programs cannot be within the .so, they
are created as required in the run-unit itself.


> >In OO COBOL a new object will dynamically create a new working-storage
> >for itself.
>
> Not if it is a FACTORY object.

That is because you can't create a new factory object, there is and
can be only one factory object for a class and that will create the
new objects which will have  a new Working-Storage.

Actually, looking at Fujitsu, it says that the factory object will be
created "before it is referenced" which could be anytime between the
program loading and the first  NEW for that class.
```

It could, and it should to be compliant with the standard. I first assumed it did. I was

>If the dll or .so is linked so that it loads at the same time as the

load time. 

People mistakenly say W-S is allocated at compile time. That's impossible because the
program might be executed on another machine or years after compilation.

when the process starts, or at the time of the first call? I honestly don't know. My guess
is that it's done at load time. It wouldn't be hard to find out.

```
Concerning ther statement,

 "They are allocated no later than immediately before initialization"

Let's look again at the entire paragraph which states,

"Data items and file connectors defined in the working-storage, communication, 
or file section of a source element that is not an initial program are static 
items. Also, screen item attributes in a source element that is not an initial 
program are treated as static items. These items are set to their initial state 
each time the runtime element or object containing them is set to its initial 
state, as described in 14.5.2.1.2.1, Initial state, and in 14.5.2.2, Initial 
state of object data. They are allocated no later than immediately before 
initialization and persist to the first of the following:
- the end of the run unit,
- the execution of a CANCEL statement of a program that directly or indirectly 
contains the items,
- the end of the object's life cycle in the case of object data."

It seems to me that Robert is assuming that "before initialization" is PROGRAM 
initialization, but in context it is clear that the "they" is the individual 
data items.  It is also clear that THEIR "persistance" in this context ends with 
a CANCEL and that the next time the same program is CALLed, this same 
"initialization" (and allocation before) can occur.

Again, all of this refers to what the Standard says (which is minimal) about wht 
type of allocation is done for what it calls "static" items and does not imply 
how many (if any) existing compiler/run-times do storage allocatin as a part of 
setting to the 2nd through N-th time of "initial state".
```

```
On Fri, 14 Mar 2008 14:29:05 GMT, "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote:

>Concerning ther statement,
>
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>"initialization" (and allocation before) can occur.

The question for THIS particular thread is whether "The standard says working-storage is
allocated statically." versus whether working-storage items can be reallocated dynamically
while the program is running. 

The standard calls them "static items" without defining the phrase. Dictionaries define
static and its Latin root statis as motionless, fixed. It seems clear to me that a static
data item is one whose address does not change during the item's life. By contrast, a
dynamic item is mobile in memory.

>Again, all of this refers to what the Standard says (which is minimal) about what 
>type of allocation is done for what it calls "static" items and does not imply 
>how many (if any) existing compiler/run-times do storage allocation as a part of 
>setting to the 2nd through N-th time of "initial state".

The 02 standard says under CANCEL:

"NOTE It is neither prohibited nor required that the storage of the specified program be
freed by the execution of a CANCEL statement."

I wish standards writers didn't embelish substantive statements with "note", giving the
impression the statements are parenthetical asides. ISO should employ copy editors to
clean up awkward techie writing style.
```

```
Actually, ISO (ITTF specifically) is very careful about the use of "NOTE".  By 
definition, in an ISO Standard anything included in a "NOTE" (or an example) is 
"non-normative".  Therefore, the point of that NOTE is "informative" and is 
simply confirming the fact that there is NO normative text telling implementers 
what they are required to do with storage when a CANCEL occurs.

If you are interested in the ISO drafting rules, I think I can find an online 
reference for you.  (It deals with things like "notes"; use of "can" "may" 
"shall" "will" etc)
```

- **References:** `<HAXAj.5290$FE.820@fe05.news.easynews.com> <47D56BE4.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <m1pbt3hf9l197putsqlr2pvr1iccpgv7ka@4ax.com> <dDzBj.440419$Ib6.70764@fe03.news.easynews.com> <f6eet3depq6ugth6br7r2mk1hd5u9uiiqk@4ax.com> <63p2sfF28n6tgU1@mid.individual.net> <38kft3t3h52mm32b1vekgqe1qu98l7l1la@4ax.com> <63q361F28e93nU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Mar 13, 2:12 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> "Robert" <n...@e.mail> wrote in message
>
…[61 more quoted lines elided]…
> maximum possible.

Whether that is true or not is an implementation issue. A particular
compiler _could_ make it truly dynamic, but the performance penalty
would be unacceptable.

In any case, for file records it _IS_ true. The size of the record on
tape/disk really does depend on the count field.

The problem with Robert's claims(this time) is that ODO is used for
two different things. It controls the size of records in files, and it
is used to limit SORT and SEARCH. The first use is a bad thing because
there shouldn't be arrays in file records, but when tapes were all one
had it was a useful way of having sub-records. The complications of
having multiple ODO led to sites banning its use, quite rightly, when
disks became available and a separate file could be used for them.

While it may be true that SEARCH ALL is fractionally faster using ODO
and the recent ability to SORT a table requires it,  these are seldom
used. Most of my tables are used sequentially because they hold groups
of data, they are lists not arrays. I can't recall ever bothering with
a SEARCH ALL. In most applications of requiring stuff by key I have
used the indexed file directly rather than load it all into an array
and SEARCH for it.

In any case SEARCH ALL still works without ODO, just fill the unused
portion with high values <shrug>. My tests do show that with Fujitsu
the ODO array access is fractionally slower, but only by 1%, and that
is with the depending on set fixed at maximum.

It is all to do with designing the system right in the first place
rather than fretting over a few milliseconds.

>
> > The standard says working-storage is allocated
…[65 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."
```

If the design is for a given volume of data and processing, one set of
simple algorithms may be more than adequate to meet organizational
needs but once that volume is far exceeded, adjustments may need to be
made.  Most times a few milliseconds are not important but then there
are the cases where the extra effort actually is meaningful.  Right in
the first place assumes that enough is known or even can be learned
within a reasonable amount of time to define right.  A system designed
for a single division may be a disaster when extended to a whole
company for a number of reasons.

```


<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:frarum$dv$1@reader2.panix.com...
> In article <8e2ht31ns17o29dk38ekb362o3v4el8p4q@4ax.com>,
> Clark F Morris  <cfmpublic@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>
This reminded me of your quote about getting fired for being wrong and 
getting fired for being right...

One of the earliest contract jobs I ever did was as a front end programmer 
on a new Banking Forex system being installed in London. (late 1970s). It 
was in a previously unknown (the machine had not yet been built for 
production) Assembler for the IBM 3790. We tested our programs under an 
emulator running on a system 370, and taught ourselves the language from the 
LRM.

The system had been "designed" (I use the term in the loosest possible 
sense)  by a very highly paid consultant from Arthur Andersen (remember them 
:-)). Unfortunately, said bright spark had not bothered to even think about 
transaction volumes... It became apparent very quickly that the new machines 
would never be able to handle the transactions required. I knew it and 
another colleague (Irish) agreed with me. Conversation went as follows:

Me: This thing would need to run 36 hours a day to deal with current 
transactions, never mind future ones.

Paddy: I know.

Me: Shouldn't we tell them?  [I was in my 30s and inexperienced as a 
contractor...]

Paddy: Probably, but I wouldn't advise it. [He was much more experienced in 
contracting...]

Me: Jesus! It will be a disaster! I'm gonna tell them.

Paddy: I'll back your position if I'm asked to, but I won't raise it with 
them.

I did,was dragged over the coals, my credibility was challenged (fortunately 
I had the printouts to show why I believed there was a problem), and my card 
was immediately marked by Arthur Andersen as a "troublemaker". My contract 
was not renewed. (Paddy's was... :-))

Your Sainted Grandmother was certainly right about that, Doc. :-)

Pete.
```

```
On Thu, 13 Mar 2008 02:12:39 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
>news:38kft3t3h52mm32b1vekgqe1qu98l7l1la@4ax.com...
…[43 more quoted lines elided]…
>No comment?

I said Micro Focus was wrong in the Myth Busters thread, six months ago. I substantiated
each point with the evidence of at least one timing test. 

Belief trumps evidence in the land of Cobol. I mentioned it now not to retread the same
ground but to use those faith-based rebuttals as evidence that some (most?) Cobol
programmers are opposed to dynamic structures. 

Now that the shoe is on the other foot, I'm wrong again. 

This is like arguing with your wife. You can't win; no matter what you say is wrong.
That's because the argument is not about facts, it's about feelings. 

At least wives don't claim to be professional.

>>>I don't use it because I don't believe it saves anything and it just adds
>>>overhead (although I realy don't care about that) and complexity (although 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>maximum possible.

The SIZE of the array depends on the count field. The LENGTH of the array is the maximum. 

Suppose you're flying a kite and someone asks the size of the tether string. You say 100
meters. Then he asks for the length of your tether string. You say 1,000 meters. He says
you're wasteful, why are you spending 1,000 meters of string to fly a kite 100 meters out?
You say it's more efficient to use one string for all kite flights (under 1,000 meters)
than to have a 100 meter string, a 200 meter string, ... and a 1,000 meter string. The
total ensamble would consume 5,500 meters of string. 

Being a business graduate, he says you could save string expense by RENTING a string of
the required length and returning it when not in use. Even though renting costs twice as
much as buying, you're not wasting string. 

Thus was born the idea of contract programmers. Expense is doubled but no one is idle.

On the other hand, if you're in the business of selling string or computers, you want to
encourage fixed partitions and preallocation, because it sells more hardware. IBM are the
experts on this. I once saw a survey of mainframe disk utilization that showed 80% of the
space was unused due to fixed allocation at about six levels -- disk, space group, file,
FBA, fixed length record padded with filler, fixed length fields padded with spaces.

I wonder how parking lots at IBM are managed. Do you suppose each parking space has a
person's name?

>> The standard says working-storage is allocated
>> statically.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>standard says is really irrelevant.

Micro Focus has little standard balloons on every clause.  

>>Actually, it can save memory, but only if YOU dynamically allocate the ODO
>> structures. This seldom comes up in ODO discussions. I mentioned it when I 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>supposed to dynamically allocate these ODO structures?

I've been doing it heavily, all the time, for over twenty years. 

1. call 'malloc' using by value length of FOO returning mem-pointer  (every compiler & OS)

2. call 'CBL_ALLOC_MEM' using mem-pointer, by value length of FOO, flags  (Micro Focus)
2a. call 'M$ALLOC' using length of FOO, mem-pointer  (Acucobol)

3. allocate FOO returning mem-pointer

4. invoke FOO "new" returning object-pointer

>>>Now, your point about sorts on ODO arrays being faster is an interesting
>>>one, but, as I have never used an ODO array in memory and therefore have 
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>don't care... :-)

Compute log2(length) to get the number of looks your way. Compute log2(size) to get the
number of looks with ODO. If your calculator doesn't have log2, use ln(x) * 1.44.

>ODOs must be calculated at run time (even for fixed references); fixed 
>length can be calculated at compile time, for fixed references. I would 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>solution is not for me...

As I said, it's about feelings.

>>What part of speed do you find complex?
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>in applications which spend most of their time waiting for IO to complete...

That's because you're hitting the database to look up common reference codes instead of
searching for them in tables.  <ducking>
```

…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> searching for them in tables.  <ducking>

…[20 more quoted lines elided]…

One can use the table as a cache. Look first in the table; if not there, try the database;
if found, add it to the table; if the table is full, reuse the oldest entry. In the days
of slow computers, I did that often. Now I let the database worry about cache management.

>I know there was a time when you ONLY posted such sweeping statements and

>hackles to rise, they are simply logically untrue, by virtue of their 
>sweeping nature. (If there is one case where Belief DOESN'T trump evidence 
>in COBOL land then your premise fails, similarly with the second statement.)

They're leaky abstractions, not syllogistic proofs. 

"In an epistemological sense, the human concept of any abstraction is always an
implementation of deeper abstractions, represented in mental concepts and verbal
statements used to convey them. There is no generally agreed-upon deepest layer of
abstraction. Much (if not most) of the field of philosophy is predicated upon searching
for such a layer.

The idea that all sufficiently interesting abstractions are leaky thus has profound
philosophical ramifications.

Consider the field of physics. A proton is an abstraction which is implemented, on a
deeper level, by a set of quarks. The extent to which the nature of the quarks "leaks"
through to the nature of the proton appears to depend largely on context such as
temperature. However, there's no doubt that the quark implementation leaks through;
otherwise, it would not be possible to know about quarks. Humans only know they exist
based on the extent to which they leak through higher-level abstractions.

In this way, the Law of Leaky Abstractions is a tautology. Any abstraction that isn't
leaky cannot be distinguished from a fundamental truth. A fundamental truth is not
properly an abstraction. Therefore, no abstractions are not leaky."
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leaky_abstraction#Philosophical_sense

>I found your post on ODO interesting and it made me think. I expressed where 
>and why I disagreed, but I don't doubt your sincerity and I don't even think 
>you are necessarily "wrong", I disagree on aesthetic grounds more than 
>anything... :-)

I LIKE ODO on aesthetic grounds. If a string is 8 bytes long, I find it more pleasing to
SAY it is 8 bytes long than to say it is 30 bytes long. If a table contains 432 entries,
the language should operate on 432 entries, not 1,000 entries. 

Why are Cobol words limited to 31 characters? Why can't they be any finite length (less
than the size of memory)? The reason is because someone, somewhere, had an aversion to
variable length strings, found them 'too complicated'.

"Procrustes (the stretcher), also known as Damastes (subduer) and Polypemon (harming
much), is a figure from Greek mythology. He was a bandit from Attica, with a stronghold in
the hills outside Eleusis. There, he had an iron bed into which he invited every passerby
to lie down. If the guest proved too tall, he would amputate the excess length; but if the
victim was found wanting in height, they were then stretched out on the rack until they
gained the required inches. ..  Procrustes continued his reign of terror until he was
captured by Theseus, who "fitted" Procrustes to his own bed and cut off his head and feet"

>You are probably right that feelings are involved here; they certainly are 
>in my case. I just don't like ODO. No really good reason for it, I just 
>avoid it like the plague. BUT, I have never had a program fail because the 
>DO wasn't set properly and I've never had any problems with ODOs because I 
>just don't use them.

I've never had to guide the user through installing a 200MB environment to run my
programs, because I just don't use .NET. :)

Last week, I passed up an opportunity to do that -- wrap Cobol into SOA services and give
them a pretty C#/AJAX face. 

>> Being a business graduate, he says you could save string expense by 
>> RENTING a string of
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>the time, it certainly isn't... :-)

Doing the job is 50% programming and 50% process overhead. Doing the job in half the time
would require cutting programming time to zero.

Last Sunday, Carl Ichan said on TV (60 Minutes) that he could improve productivity in any
big company by 30%, just by eliminating waste. By coincidence, the next morning I heard a
high-powered technology VP say she could improve productivity in any development shop by
50%, by good management. 

Do I hear 70%?  60%? Going once, twice, SOLD to the lady holding bamboo splints.  

>>>I know of no way in COBOL (apart from CICS and Assembler GETMAIN calls on
>>>IBM mainframes) that can DYNAMICALLY allocate storage. So how exactly am I
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>to C language facilities, directly from COBOL?

Think outside the Cobol box. You're not limited to Cobol functions, you can call ANY
function that follows the XP/ELF/ABI standard. NetCOBOL under Win XP has access to
hundreds, if not thousands, of useful functions. They're only a CALL away.

>
> In this way, the Law of Leaky Abstractions is a tautology. Any abstraction 
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> variable length strings, found them 'too complicated'.

Oh Dear, there you go assuming again....

I know the answer to this one, because it is one of the questions I asked on 
my first COBOL course in 1967...

It has nothing to do with variable strings being too complicated for 
compiler writers to deal with.  Later I spent some time with people who had 
actually written a very popular COBOL compiler, and I even got to patch the 
compiler myself so it would do what was needed for the site where I was 
employed. All of this experience confirmed that the 31 byte limit on a COBOL 
name was nothing to do with variable strings being too complicated. I'm not 
going to tell you what the real reason was, but I can give you this clue:

At the time COBOL was developed the 8 bit byte had not yet become the 
industry standard. Different architectures used 64, 32, or 24 bit words, 
based on a 6 bit character. COBOL ran on these machines just as it did on 8 
bit byte architectures.
>
> "Procrustes (the stretcher), also known as Damastes (subduer) and 
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

…[6 more quoted lines elided]…

…[23 more quoted lines elided]…

No, you are wrong, again. The 31 character limit is that the _first_
31 characters of user defined words are used. Anything after 31 is
ignored. So if some legacy code has used this by having longer names,
but has been uncaring that the additional characters are consistent,
then it will impact that code.

```


"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
news:n80nt3hh9uebhvl0g5knjnsmcdmfauj7o6@4ax.com...
> On Sat, 15 Mar 2008 17:39:08 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" 
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> unprofessional.

Thank you. I can honestly say no-one has ever called my workplace writing 
"unprofessional" (at least, not to my face...:-)).

I saw your post where this had happened to you over a Business Requirement. 
I strongly support your desire for clear English.

Your colleague(s) saw fit to re-write your SOR with more buzzwords and 
jargon. This indicates that what you did was just too general or was more 
general than is normally the case on that site. Sometimes we use jargon 
because it communicates unequivocally; sometimes it is just part of 
protecting the mystique, in the interest of job security... :-)

At the end of the day, it really doesn't matter what you or your 
colleague(s) thought about your document; what matters is what the person 
who had to use it thought. A Statement of Requirements needs to communicate 
clearly. Using plain English is a good way to acomplish this (in fact, using 
plain English is a good way to communicate in most situations...), but, if 
(and only if) the document was perceived BY THE USER as failing to 
communicate, or leaving things unclear, then it must be amended, or (and 
preferably, in my opinion) some face to face discussion between the author 
and the user needs to take place.

If one of my colleagues picked up a document of mine and started editing it 
to fit his own perceptions of how such a document should be written, I'd 
probably beat him about the head and neck with it :-). (Perhaps the implicit 
recognition of this, is why no-one's ever done it... :-))

Seriously, if someone felt the need to edit what I wrote, I'd definitely sit 
down and try to understand why they did.

The need for written requirements is a hangover from the Waterfall and is 
more about "cover your arse" and control than it is about communicating. In 
more dynamic and fluid environments (like Agile...) face to face 
communication trumps it easily. If you work in an environment where you 
EXPECT requirements to change (even during development), and actually 
WELCOME that change, you won't be writing down requirements. (You WILL be 
writing down timeboxed, prioritised goals (agreed jointly with the Business) 
that will become deliverables out of the timebox...)

(I've just been writing about exactly this in the book I am finishing. There 
is much more to it than I can elucidate here.)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** Robert <no@e.mail>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<otgqt3p795elql42v61deisgj6u9ncpnuf@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<63p2sfF28n6tgU1@mid.individual.net> <38kft3t3h52mm32b1vekgqe1qu98l7l1la@4ax.com> <63q361F28e93nU1@mid.individual.net> <cm4ht3t7o5mpec667tfe24geucbtor16ut@4ax.com> <63smgfF28gk93U1@mid.individual.net> <2eojt39d64fen4a9oemc1aorp500lfh92t@4ax.com> <63uuf7F29iaqiU1@mid.individual.net> <ks8mt39al9dmh8tjiba170g7tppdkgb2sp@4ax.com> <64127eF2a3oppU1@mid.individual.net> <n80nt3hh9uebhvl0g5knjnsmcdmfauj7o6@4ax.com> <6432qaF289k08U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Sun, 16 Mar 2008 12:01:28 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

>"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
>news:n80nt3hh9uebhvl0g5knjnsmcdmfauj7o6@4ax.com...

>I saw your post where this had happened to you over a Business Requirement. 
>I strongly support your desire for clear English.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>protecting the mystique, in the interest of job security... :-)

Technical language can give a false illusion of accuracy. My Business Requirement is the
fourth attempt to solve a problem. The first three were precisely written and implemented,
but produced wrong answers because they made false assumptions about the real world. 
One assumption was that a US wireline telephone number can be geographically located by
taking the first six digits of the number and looking up the location of the central
office containing the switch handling that exchange. Can you figure out why that sometimes
gives wrong answers? Hint: old numbers are in area code 909, medium aged ones are in 915,
newer ones are in 951. The answer is not call forwarding nor foreign exchange.

Technical language is sometimes used to silence semi-technical subject matter experts.
When the problem is described in language they can understand, they embarrass us by
bringing up facts we don't know, for example legal issues. That's what they're supposed to
do. When the spec is in technical language, they agree to everything rather than admit
they don't understand it.

>At the end of the day, it really doesn't matter what you or your 
>colleague(s) thought about your document; what matters is what the person 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>and the user needs to take place.

Face to face is impractical when parties are all over the planet. We do in-person meetings
via NetMeeting and telephone. When nontechnical management is involved, we add closed
circuit TV. NetMeeting has become the norm in large US companies. It is actually MORE
effective than a conference room because everyone can SEE what you're talking about.
You're not limited to PowerPoint slides nor are you limited to text, as in this forum.

Take for example code review. In the old days it was done with hardcopy listings and
colored marker pens in a conference room. Now it is done with Beyond Compare pointed at
the code repository. Participants, who are often in different cities or countries, are all
looking at the leader's desktop (screen). When another participant wants to illustrate a
technical point, the leader shifts focus to that person's desktop. 

Conducting meetings in this environment requires virtuosity with software tools. Effective
leaders do not fumble.

>If one of my colleagues picked up a document of mine and started editing it 
>to fit his own perceptions of how such a document should be written, I'd 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>down and try to understand why they did.

Turn on the audit feature in Word. They can even add sticky notes to explain the rationale
for a change. 

>The need for written requirements is a hangover from the Waterfall and is 
>more about "cover your arse" and control than it is about communicating. In 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>that will become deliverables out of the timebox...)

All too often I work on code that lacks explanation of WHY it's doing something. Sure, I
can see WHAT it's doing, but that's not enough. I write comments to explain non-obvious
background, sometimes whole paragraphs. On one project I put hot links in the code, tying
it to the technical spec. The code reviewer deleted them, said they were "unprofessional".
I also linked the technical spec to the business spec. The documentation reviewer deleted
those links, for the same reason.

Yeah, right. Mixed case code is still considered unprofessional in mainframe shops. At
least everyone knows it's a dying culture.

>(I've just been writing about exactly this in the book I am finishing. There 
>is much more to it than I can elucidate here.)

Beware of advocating 1990s ideas.
```

There is no need for code review if an Agile approach is adopted. Code 
either works or it doesn't and most of what is written won't be maintained 
(I am NOT talking about COBOL development here). I find what you are 
describing "quaint"... :-) On the one hand, you sometimes belabour COBOL 
people for being stuck in the past, then you start talking about things like 
"Statement of Requirements" documents, and "code review" which are 
techniques from the very era you despise...:-)

>>If one of my colleagues picked up a document of mine and started editing 
>>it
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> for a change.

I use it all the time when reviewing documents.
>
>>The need for written requirements is a hangover from the Waterfall and is
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> can see WHAT it's doing, but that's not enough.

This has been a problem since people started programming computers. COBOL 
was an attempt to address it by using meaningful names and English-like 
verbs and syntax, but, as has been observed here before, some people can 
write Fortran in ANY language... :-)

It is only a problem if the code needs maintenance.

On non-OO COBOL sites (much more so than on sites where OO COBOL is used as 
part of a component based approach), the code DOES need maintenance, so an 
overview, background document, or specific notes in the code itself need to 
be provided. (One of the problems with all of these "good ideas" is that 
they require ongoing commitment and investment...If documentation is not 
kept up to date, it is almost useless, and people have no faith in it, so 
don't use it. Pretty pointless writing documents that people can't/won't 
use... More modern approaches like Agile, simply obviate the need for it.


> I write comments to explain non-obvious
> background, sometimes whole paragraphs. On one project I put hot links in 
> the code, tying
> it to the technical spec. The code reviewer deleted them, said they were 
> "unprofessional".

It seems that "unprofessional" is a word that gets used a lot on the site 
where you work. Perhaps its use needs to be challenged. In what way is tying 
code back to a tech spec. "unprofessional"?  It could be problematic if the 
code gets changed and the tech spec. doesn't, but that is hardly a problem 
of professionalism...Suppose it wasn't a hot link, just a reference; would 
that be "unprofessional" too?

> I also linked the technical spec to the business spec. The documentation 
> reviewer deleted
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> least everyone knows it's a dying culture.

Maybe. As long as there are managers who see management as being about 
control, rather than facilitation, the culture won't die...
>
>>(I've just been writing about exactly this in the book I am finishing. 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Beware of advocating 1990s ideas.

Me?! Never!!!... I liked the 60s... :-)

Nevertheless, I remember wearing floral shirts with high collars to work (I 
wasn't the only one :-)) and gentle things like being given a flower and a 
smile as I walked down Queen Street at lunchtime, the girl who used to sing 
while I played guitar for her and who made me a necklace out of dried beans 
(which I wore for a long time until it broke), the sit ins in Albert Park, 
the "smoke ins" on Waiheke Island, the naked dancing on Rakino, and the 
music... Man, it has never been bettered.

It was "time out" from the overhanging threat of nuclear extermination and 
the Cold War, after they killed the one decent, inspiring, American who 
brought us all hope, and replaced him with a succession of  puppets to the 
Establishment and rascals, who held the fate of the World in their hands, 
and whom we never even got to vote on.

A gentler, less frenetic time, where, for a short while at least, we really 
believed that Love was the answer to everything and people were kind to each 
other without ulterior motive.

Then the 70s arrived and burst our bubble :-)

I don't live in the past, and I'm happy in 2008, but I liked the 60s...:-)

Pete.

production, its specs are moved to an archive.

had a hot link AND a copy. When you opened it, it would try to follow the link. If the
document was still there and had been updated, it updated its copy. If the link was
broken, it said so and displayed the copy.

>By the results. How else would you know whether code is right?

I know by reading the code and understanding what it says. Beyond trivial cases, it's
impossible to test all possible inputs to a program, because the number of combinations
grows exponentially. That's why truth tables failed. 

Say the program is testing three integers. To test every combination of positive values,
you would need  9903520314283042199192993792 test cases. If it's testing five alphanumeric
bytes, you would need 10000000000 test cases.

>> users? Rules must be

>to be updated; if they are put into program code, the tool that documents 
>the code finds the changes automatically.

Why can't you do that with Cobol or any other language?

>> Have you ever seen links in program source code?
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>links, to anchor points within the program itself.

That's the way it should be. I'm pleased to read C# people got it right.

"Robert" <no@e.mail> wrote in message 
news:0hfut3ll67seud23rj0jbv0herlrotqpeq@4ax.com...
> On Tue, 18 Mar 2008 11:57:35 +1300, "Pete Dashwood" 
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> bytes, you would need 10000000000 test cases.

So, you test the range limits for each integer and do a number of random 
tests, then apply last month's live data to it....

What you are saying would require you to read and understand every line of 
production code on a givern site. Given there could be several million 
lines, this will take longer than your lifetime, and it might not be wrong 
anyway...:-)  I prefer the pragmatic approach. It is tested to the 
satisfaction of all interested parties and if it fails after that, it gets 
fixed immediately. I am not suggesting that code should not be written 
carefully or thoughtfully. I am saying it can evolve, rather than be 
designed from day one. (At least, it can if you don't have to keep 
maintaining it...and you won't have to, if working functionality is properly 
encapsulated. If code is NOT integrated, but is separated by layers and 
components, it DOESN'T need the same levels of maintenance and testing.) OO 
sites know this; it is much harder for non-OO sites to grasp.

>
>>> How do you know what the rules for those decisions are? By talking to
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> Why can't you do that with Cobol or any other language?

You can (and I do...) with C#.

>
>>> Have you ever seen links in program source code?
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>

It is an upward leap from COBOL. But it had the benefit of 50 years of 
hindsight, which COBOL did not.

(I still regard CODASYL with awe; they did an incredible job, given the 
knowledge and circumstances of the time. It is a bloody shame their noble 
vision was screwed over by subsequent guardians of COBOL.)

Also, the author of it is a real world programmer and not an ivory tower 
academic. I have been very impressed with his attitude and approach in 
interviews and instructional videos where he talks enthusiastically about 
it, and where he shares his vision of where he wants it to go. He doesn't 
have to battle a moribund standards authority, like COBOL either.

I still can't believe it is free... it's like you picked a sports car and 
the guy on the lot said: "OK, we really want to get people driving these, so 
you can have it for nothing..." :-)

Pete.

On Sun, 16 Mar 2008 12:01:28 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>The need for written requirements is a hangover from the Waterfall and is 
>more about "cover your arse" and control than it is about communicating. In 
>more dynamic and fluid environments (like Agile...) face to face

customers are in several different cities.    I like to follow up the
face-to-face meetings with written documentation for their approval.

things - even though they were talking with each other as well as with
me - and are conversant with each other's jargon and needs.   The
written "cover your arse" document is important for everybody to check
early.

```


"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message 
news:ph1tt3lausm7o41155g3o8at98amou8b06@4ax.com...
> On Sun, 16 Mar 2008 12:01:28 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> customers are in several different cities.

Yes, they are, just like they are missed in documents as well... That's why 
we use interaction and iteration. Nevertheless, I find face-to-face is much 
better than documents. A group of people, with stakes in the outcome, 
working together, can communicate more completely face-to-face than they can 
through written documents. (And there is an audit trail of decisions taken 
by them, and why). Although having people co-located is very helpful, it 
isn't essential. In this day and age, with the tools we have available, 
distances really don't matter so much.

There is much more behind my comments than I have hinted at here.  You will 
need to read the book... :-) Or, better still, wait for the movie... :-)


> I like to follow up the
> face-to-face meetings with written documentation for their approval.
>
Yes, that does no harm. I use an audit trail of decisions taken in meetings.

> Sometimes I find out that different users were expecting different
> things - even though they were talking with each other as well as with
> me - and are conversant with each other's jargon and needs.   The
> written "cover your arse" document is important for everybody to check
> early.

Only in the absence of other means to confirm agreement and communication. 
Like I said, there is much more to this.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Frank Swarbrick" <Frank.Swarbrick@efirstbank.com>
- **Date:** 2008-03-12T13:36:38-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<47D7DC66.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`
- **References:** `<HAXAj.5290$FE.820@fe05.news.easynews.com> <47D56BE4.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <m1pbt3hf9l197putsqlr2pvr1iccpgv7ka@4ax.com> <dDzBj.440419$Ib6.70764@fe03.news.easynews.com> <f6eet3depq6ugth6br7r2mk1hd5u9uiiqk@4ax.com> <63p2sfF28n6tgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
>>> On 3/11/2008 at 10:01 PM, in message
<63p2sfF28n6tgU1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> No they don't. But they can vary in length, WITHOUT needing ODO... 
> VARCHAR 
> columns are implemented the way ODO SHOULD have been... transparently to 
> the 
> user.

This is exactly one of the reasons that Cobol should have dynamic length
fields.  Currently you have to define a "VARCHAR" in Cobol like this:

01  A-VARCHAR-STRING.
    49  A-VARCHAR-STRING-LEN   PIC S9(4) COMP-5.
    49  A-VARCHAR-STRING-TXT   PIC X(1000).

EXEC SQL
    SELECT A_VARCHAR_STRING
    INTO :A-VARCHAR-STRING
    FROM FRANK.TABLE1
END-EXEC

DISPLAY 'A_VARCHAR_STRING = "' A-VARCHAR-STRING-TXT(1:A-VARCHAR-STRING-LEN)
'"'.

Yuck!!  I would much rather have:

01  A-VARCHAR-STRING   PIC X ANY LENGTH
                       LIMIT IS 1000 
                       PREFIXED BY BINARY-SHORT.

EXEC SQL
    SELECT A_VARCHAR_STRING
    INTO :A-VARCHAR-STRING
    FROM FRANK.TABLE1
END-EXEC

DISPLAY 'A_VARCHAR_STRING = "' A-VARCHAR-STRING '"'.

Though to be honest the data definition is much too long, but I could live
with it just to have this feature.

Frank
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: J4 - presentation/discussion on "Future of the COBOL Standard"

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2008-03-12T20:06:50+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<uJWBj.21736$us.5077@fe04.news.easynews.com>`
- **References:** `<HAXAj.5290$FE.820@fe05.news.easynews.com> <47D56BE4.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com> <m1pbt3hf9l197putsqlr2pvr1iccpgv7ka@4ax.com> <dDzBj.440419$Ib6.70764@fe03.news.easynews.com> <f6eet3depq6ugth6br7r2mk1hd5u9uiiqk@4ax.com> <63p2sfF28n6tgU1@mid.individual.net> <47D7DC66.6F0F.0085.0@efirstbank.com>`

```
If the proposed revision:

A) limited ANY LENGTH items to the 01-level, then it might work better

B) made any of the BINARY-xxxx fields something other than processor dependent

C) didn't try and support both "direct" and "indirect" storage of such items,

Then, Yes, I would say that it might be a useful feature.  As currently defined, 
it just has BUNCHES of problems (both in the specification and probable 
performance hits).
```
